The Anti-Cherry-Picking Constraint becomes fully clear when applied step by step to actual reasoning patterns. The purpose of these examples is not only to show that the claims fail, but to demonstrate exactly where and why they fail within the structure defined in this chapter.

### Example 1: **Quantum-Based Claims About Reality**

Step 1: **Selection**

A claim focuses on quantum mechanics, specifically on interpretive ambiguity or the role of measurement.

Step 2: **Isolation**

Quantum phenomena are treated as if they define the nature of reality independently of other domains.

Step 3: **Elevation**

The quantum domain is treated as more fundamental and therefore more authoritative than all higher-level domains.

Step 4: **Exclusion**

Domains with stronger closure, including chemistry, biology, and neuroscience, are ignored or treated as secondary.

Step 5: **Extension**

A global claim is made, such as “consciousness creates reality” or “reality is fundamentally undefined.”

Failure Analysis

* The claim relies on a domain with incomplete mechanistic closure  
* It ignores domains with stronger phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure  
* It violates cross-scale integration  
* It fails the inclusion requirement  
* It fails the resolution requirement

This is not a weak theory. It is disqualified prior to evaluation.

### Example 2: **Consciousness Independent of the Brain**

Step 1: **Selection**

A claim appeals to subjective experience or philosophical ambiguity about consciousness.

Step 2: **Isolation**

Subjective experience is treated as sufficient to define the nature of consciousness.

Step 3: **Elevation**

The subjective domain is granted authority beyond its level of closure.

Step 4: **Exclusion**

Neuroscience is ignored, dismissed, or treated as irrelevant.

Step 5: **Extension**

A conclusion is drawn that consciousness exists independently of the brain.

Failure Analysis

* Neuroscience demonstrates strong phenomenon closure and substantial mechanistic closure  
* The claim does not achieve closure parity  
* It does not provide a mechanism that accounts for brain–mind relationships  
* It does not integrate with biological knowledge  
* It violates system-level consistency

This claim does not qualify as a theory. It is structurally invalid.

### Example 3: **Rejection of Evolution**

Step 1: **Selection**

A claim focuses on isolated observations or perceived gaps in evolutionary explanations.

Step 2: **Isolation**

These observations are treated as if they are sufficient to challenge evolutionary theory.

Step 3: **Elevation**

The selected anomalies are granted authority beyond their evidential scope.

Step 4: **Exclusion**

The full body of evidence across genetics, ecology, and geology is ignored.

Step 5: **Extension**

A conclusion is drawn that evolution does not occur.

Failure Analysis

* Evolution exhibits strong phenomenon closure across observed patterns  
* It exhibits strong mechanistic closure through genetic and ecological processes  
* The alternative claim does not achieve closure parity  
* It does not produce predictive models that match or exceed evolutionary theory  
* It fails integration across domains

This claim is not a competing theory. It is disqualified.

These examples demonstrate a consistent structure. In each case:

* A domain is selected  
* Constraints are removed  
* A conclusion is extended  
* The system is violated

The result is always the same.

The claim does not fail because it is controversial.

It does not fail because it lacks popularity or consensus.

It fails because it violates the structural requirements of scientific reasoning.

Selective reasoning does not produce alternative explanations. It produces invalid ones.

## **Why Selective Reasoning Always Fails**

Selective reasoning does not fail by accident. It fails by necessity. The structure of the error guarantees its invalidity regardless of the specific claim being made.

This can be demonstrated at multiple levels.

### **Logical Failure**

A claim that excludes relevant premises cannot produce a valid conclusion. If a theory depends on ignoring domains that bear directly on the phenomenon, it violates basic principles of reasoning. The conclusion is not supported by the full set of relevant information. It is derived from an incomplete and biased subset.

### **Epistemic Failure**

Knowledge is distributed across domains with varying levels of phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure. Selective reasoning disrupts this structure by elevating domains with weaker closure and ignoring domains with stronger closure. This reverses the hierarchy of knowledge. What is least established is treated as most authoritative.

### **Structural Failure**

Scientific explanation requires integration across scales. Selective reasoning prevents integration by removing domains that would impose constraints. A theory that cannot integrate across the full structure of knowledge is not incomplete. It is structurally incoherent.

### **Validation Failure**

A theory must satisfy system-level conditions before it can be evaluated. Selective reasoning violates those conditions. It fails prior to any assessment of predictive power, explanatory scope, or empirical support.

These failures are not independent. They reinforce one another. Logical incompleteness leads to epistemic distortion. Epistemic distortion leads to structural inconsistency. Structural inconsistency prevents validation.

The result is unavoidable:

Selective reasoning cannot produce a valid theory.

This is not contingent on the content of the claim. It is determined by the form of the reasoning.

## **Final Enforcement Statements**

The Anti-Cherry-Picking Constraint must be enforced without exception. It is not a guideline for improving reasoning. It is a condition that determines whether reasoning qualifies as scientific.

To eliminate ambiguity, the constraint can be stated in multiple equivalent forms:

* A theory that ignores relevant domains is disqualified.  
* A claim that depends on selective evidence cannot be generalized.  
* A theory that violates system-level consistency fails prior to evaluation.  
* A domain with weaker closure cannot be used to override domains with stronger closure.  
* A theory must satisfy all relevant constraints simultaneously or it does not qualify as valid.


Each of these statements reinforces the same principle. There is no pathway by which selective reasoning can produce a legitimate explanation.

This also establishes a boundary on interpretation:

Interpretation is not free to select its inputs.

It is constrained by the full structure of knowledge.

Any attempt to bypass this constraint does not expand explanatory freedom. It collapses it.

## **Integration with the System of Validation**

The Anti-Cherry-Picking Constraint is not an isolated rule. It is integrated with the broader system established in this chapter. The distinction between evidence of phenomena and evidence for theories ensures that explanations must be validated independently. The scale constraint ensures that no domain may override another without closure parity. The integration requirement ensures that a theory must align across all relevant scales.

The Anti-Cherry-Picking Constraint enforces all of these simultaneously.

A claim that violates this constraint fails to integrate across scales, fails to respect closure relationships, and fails to satisfy validation requirements. As a result, it cannot achieve phenomenon closure or mechanistic closure at the level required for theoretical status.

It fails across all three axes of evaluation: epistemic structure, ontological scale, and closure.

This leads to a final conclusion:

#### **A claim that relies on selective reasoning cannot become a validated theory under any circumstances.**

It does not matter how compelling it appears within a restricted domain. It does not matter how intuitively appealing it may be. It does not matter how widely it is discussed.

If it cannot survive full-system evaluation, it does not qualify as a theory.

### **Closing Transition**

With the Anti-Cherry-Picking Constraint established, the structure of valid reasoning is now fully defined.

A theory must be supported by evidence, achieve closure, respect scale, integrate across domains, and satisfy system-level consistency.

Any violation of these conditions results in disqualification.

The next section brings these constraints together into a final synthesis. It defines the discipline of interpretation as a unified system and establishes the hierarchy that governs how knowledge is evaluated and applied.

## **The Structure of Valid Interpretation**

The previous sections established the constraints that govern scientific reasoning. This section makes those constraints operational. It defines how interpretation must proceed in practice. It is not a summary. It is a decision structure.

Interpretation is not free-form reasoning. It is a constrained process that must follow a specific order. Each step introduces conditions that must be satisfied before moving to the next. If those conditions are not met, the claim does not advance. It is disqualified.

This structure prevents the collapse identified at the beginning of the chapter. It ensures that claims are evaluated in relation to epistemic level, ontological scale, and closure before any interpretive extension is allowed.

The goal is not to restrict inquiry. It is to enforce validity. A claim that follows this structure can be evaluated as a theory. A claim that does not is rejected prior to evaluation.

## **The Evaluation Order**

All claims must be evaluated in the following order. This order is not interchangeable. Each step constrains the next.

### **1\. Identify the Epistemic Level**

Determine whether the claim is operating at the level of raw data, phenomena, or theory.

This establishes what kind of statement is being made and what standards apply to it. A measurement is not an explanation. A pattern is not a mechanism. A theory is not validated by observation alone.

If the epistemic level is misidentified, all subsequent reasoning fails. A theoretical claim treated as if it were a direct observation inherits authority it has not earned. A pattern treated as if it were an explanation bypasses the requirements of theory. The entire structure collapses at the first step.

### **2\. Identify the Ontological Scale**

Determine the domain of reality to which the claim belongs.

This includes the level of organization at which the claim is made, such as physics, chemistry, biology, or cognition. Epistemic level and ontological scale are independent variables. Data, phenomena, and theories exist at every scale.

If the scale is not specified, the scope of the claim is undefined. A statement about quantum systems does not automatically apply to biological systems. A claim about cognition does not override established neuroscience. Without scale identification, there is no boundary on interpretation, and the claim becomes structurally ambiguous.

### **3\. Assess Closure Level**

Determine the degree of phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure within the relevant domain.

This establishes how much interpretive freedom exists. Where phenomenon closure is strong, what happens is not in question. Where mechanistic closure is strong, how it happens is well established. Where closure is weak, interpretation must remain limited.

Closure is scale-relative. It must be assessed within the domain identified in the previous step. A domain may exhibit strong phenomenon closure and weak mechanistic closure simultaneously. That profile defines what can and cannot be claimed.

### **4\. Check Cross-Scale Consistency**

Determine whether the claim aligns with established knowledge across all relevant scales.

A claim cannot contradict domains with equal or greater closure without achieving closure parity or superiority. All relevant domains must be included. Conflicts must be resolved, not ignored.

If the claim fails this step, it is disqualified. It does not proceed. There is no partial validity at this stage. A theory that cannot survive cross-scale consistency is not a theory in development. It is structurally invalid.

### **5\. Evaluate Theory Performance**

If the claim survives the previous steps, it may be evaluated as a theory.

At this stage, the standard criteria apply. The theory must generate predictions, be falsifiable, provide a mechanism, and integrate across domains. It must outperform competing explanations.

Only after passing structural constraints does a claim qualify for theoretical evaluation.

## **Applied Walkthrough**

To see how this structure operates in practice, consider a claim that “consciousness exists independently of the brain.”

First, the epistemic level must be identified. This is a theoretical claim. It is not raw data and not a pattern. It is an explanation about the nature of mind.

Second, the ontological scale must be identified. The claim operates at the level of cognition but has implications for neuroscience and biology.

Third, closure must be assessed. Neuroscience exhibits strong phenomenon closure and substantial mechanistic closure in the relationship between brain states and mental states.

Fourth, cross-scale consistency must be checked. The claim conflicts with established neuroscience and does not achieve closure parity. It does not provide a mechanism that accounts for brain–mind dependence.

The claim fails at this stage. It does not proceed to theory evaluation. It is disqualified before any further analysis.

This is the function of the structure. It prevents invalid claims from entering the stage of theoretical comparison by enforcing constraints at the level of form.

### **Disqualification Triggers**

A claim is disqualified immediately if it violates any of the following conditions:

It violates closure by treating unresolved phenomena as if they were established or by introducing explanations that lack mechanistic support.

It violates the scale constraint by using a domain with weaker closure to reinterpret or override a domain with stronger closure.

It relies on selective reasoning by including favorable domains while excluding those that would impose constraints.

It lacks predictive power, falsifiability, or mechanism, and therefore fails to qualify as a theory.

These are not weaknesses. They are structural failures. A claim that triggers any of these conditions does not proceed to further evaluation.

Disqualification is not a judgment of plausibility, popularity, or intuitive appeal. It is a determination that the claim fails to meet the minimum conditions required for theoretical status. A disqualified claim is not a weak theory. It is not an emerging theory. It is not a competing theory. It is not a theory at all.

This distinction must be enforced. Without it, invalid claims are allowed to enter the space of evaluation, where they can appear persuasive despite failing at the level of structure.

## **Override Conditions**

A claim may overturn or reinterpret existing knowledge only under strict conditions.

It must achieve closure parity or superiority relative to the domain it challenges. This includes both phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure.

It must provide a mechanism that accounts for the established patterns it seeks to replace.

It must integrate across all relevant domains without contradiction, demonstrating consistency with the broader structure of knowledge.

If these conditions are not met, the claim does not override existing knowledge. It fails.

Override is not achieved through reinterpretation alone. It is achieved through superior explanatory performance across the full system. The burden of proof lies entirely with the overriding claim. Established knowledge does not yield to possibility. It yields only to demonstrated superiority.

## **Interpretation Boundaries**

Interpretation is constrained by the level of closure within a domain.

Where interpretation remains unresolved, conclusions must remain limited. Speculative domains do not support global claims. They define areas of ongoing inquiry, not foundations for broad reinterpretation.

Where closure is strong, interpretation is constrained. Established phenomena and mechanisms define the boundaries within which conclusions must operate. These domains do not remain open to unrestricted reinterpretation.

This creates an asymmetry.

* Unresolved domains permit limited conclusions.  
* Established domains impose constraints on interpretation.  
* Interpretation must proceed from what is most established toward what is less certain. It cannot proceed in the opposite direction without collapsing into speculation.

This asymmetry is not optional. It is the condition that preserves coherence across domains.

### **Operational Summary**

The structure of valid interpretation can be stated as a unified process.

A claim must be correctly located within an epistemic level and an ontological scale.

Its closure must be assessed relative to that scale.

It must align with established knowledge across all relevant domains.

It must satisfy the requirements of theory validation.

If it fails at any point, it is disqualified.

This process is sequential, not optional, and not reversible. Each step constrains the next. Each failure terminates the claim. There is no pathway by which a claim can bypass structural evaluation and proceed directly to interpretation.

This transforms interpretation from an open-ended activity into a disciplined system. It ensures that reasoning remains anchored to evidence, constrained by closure, and integrated across the full structure of knowledge.

Within this system, a claim does not become valid by being persuasive.

It does not become valid by being possible.

It does not become valid by referencing legitimate domains in isolation.

It becomes valid only by satisfying the full set of constraints simultaneously.

A claim that follows this structure can be evaluated as a theory.

A claim that does not is not an alternative explanation.

It is invalid.

## **Final Synthesis: The Discipline of Scientific Reasoning**

Scientific reasoning is not defined by the accumulation of data, nor by the construction of theories in isolation. It is defined by the disciplined relationship between them. Data, phenomena, and theory form a structured system of engagement with reality, and that system does not organize itself. It is governed.

Without constraint, data is overextended, patterns are misinterpreted, and theories are treated as if they inherit authority they have not earned. The result is not the absence of information, but the breakdown of reasoning. The purpose of this chapter has been to prevent that breakdown by establishing the structural conditions under which scientific knowledge remains valid.

These conditions are not methodological preferences. They are the requirements that distinguish explanation from speculation.

Scientific knowledge begins at the level of measurement. Data establishes what is detected. It records interaction with the world under controlled conditions and provides the foundation upon which all further reasoning depends. However, data does not organize itself into meaning. It does not explain. It does not establish structure beyond detection.

When data converges across methods, conditions, and independent lines of inquiry, stable phenomena emerge. These phenomena are not singular. They exist across multiple levels of organization, from localized events to large-scale patterns to higher-order structures that themselves become objects of scientific knowledge. At this level, the question is no longer what was detected, but what reliably happens.

This distinction is critical because it introduces the first form of closure. Phenomenon closure establishes that a pattern is real, stable, and no longer in question within its domain. It does not depend on complete explanation. It defines what must be explained.

Theory operates at a fundamentally different level. It does not establish what happens. It attempts to explain why it happens. It introduces mechanisms, causal structures, and predictive frameworks. A theory is not observed. It is constructed and then evaluated. Its validity depends entirely on its performance.

This introduces the second form of closure. Mechanistic closure establishes that the processes responsible for observed patterns are sufficiently understood, testable, and integrated with other domains. Where this closure is achieved, explanation is no longer provisional at the level of basic structure.

These distinctions cannot be collapsed. Data is not theory. Patterns are not mechanisms. Explanation does not inherit the authority of observation. When these levels are conflated, interpretation detaches from evidence and begins to operate without constraint. When they are maintained, reasoning remains anchored.

This entire structure exists across ontological scales. Scientific knowledge is distributed across domains corresponding to different levels of reality. Physical systems, chemical processes, biological organization, and cognitive phenomena each introduce their own patterns, mechanisms, and explanatory frameworks. These domains are not interchangeable, and they cannot be treated as if they operate under a single uniform description.

A critical condition follows from this:

Epistemic type and ontological scale are independent variables.

Data, phenomena, and theory exist at every scale, but they are always constrained by the domain in which they are established. A pattern at one level does not automatically define behavior at another. A theory constructed within one domain does not extend beyond it without meeting the requirements of every domain it attempts to address.

This is where closure becomes the governing constraint.

Closure is a structural status of knowledge defined by two distinct conditions: the stability of patterns and the completeness of explanations. It is not a measure of confidence or consensus. It varies across scales, exists simultaneously across domains, and defines the boundaries of valid interpretation.

Where phenomenon closure is strong, what happens is no longer in question. Where mechanistic closure is strong, how it happens is well established. Where closure is incomplete, interpretation must remain limited. This is a condition of coherence.

Because closure is uneven, knowledge is not flat. It is structured by degrees of stability. This produces an unavoidable asymmetry:

Domains with stronger closure constrain domains with weaker closure.

This asymmetry defines the direction of valid reasoning. Interpretation must proceed from what is most established toward what is less certain. It cannot proceed in the opposite direction without collapsing the structure of knowledge.

From this, the scale constraint follows:

#### **A claim made at one scale cannot override established knowledge at another unless it achieves closure parity or superiority across all relevant domains.**

This includes both phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure and requires integration across scales. Without this, the claim fails at the level of structure. It does not qualify as a weaker explanation, an alternative, or a competing theory.

This constraint is reinforced by validation. A theory must generate predictions, be testable, provide mechanisms, and integrate with established knowledge. Compatibility with data is not sufficient. Performance is required. A theory must earn its status through demonstrated explanatory success.

It is further reinforced by integration. Scientific knowledge is not a collection of isolated domains. It is a unified structure in which each domain constrains the others. A theory cannot succeed locally while failing globally. It must align across all relevant scales simultaneously.

This eliminates selective reasoning.

A claim that depends on excluding relevant domains cannot qualify as a theory. A claim that survives only within a restricted subset of evidence is not partially valid. It is structurally invalid. It fails before evaluation begins.

These constraints converge into a single, non-negotiable structure.

Scientific reasoning is the coordinated application of:

* Data as measurement  
* Phenomena as stable, multi-level pattern  
* Theory as explanatory model

Governed by:

* Ontological scale  
* Closure  
* Validation  
* Cross-scale integration

Within this structure, interpretation is neither eliminated nor elevated. It is disciplined. It operates within boundaries defined by evidence, structure, and closure. It does not determine those boundaries. It is constrained by them.

This leads to the final condition that governs all claims:

A claim must align with established closure across all relevant scales and satisfy the requirements of validation and integration. If it does not, it is disqualified.

A claim does not become valid because it is conceivable.  
It does not become valid because it can be made consistent with selected data.  
It does not become valid because it introduces a novel interpretation.

It becomes valid only by satisfying the full structure of constraints simultaneously.

There is no intermediate category in which a claim that violates these constraints remains partially valid. Where the structure is satisfied, the claim can be evaluated as a theory. Where it is not, the claim does not qualify as an explanation.

It is invalid.

This is the discipline of scientific reasoning in its complete form.

It is not openness to all interpretations.  
It is not the elevation of uncertainty over established knowledge.  
It is not the collapse of distinction between observation and explanation.

It is the enforcement of structure across all levels of inquiry.

When this structure is maintained, knowledge remains coherent, cumulative, and accountable to reality. Patterns are stabilized, explanations are tested, and interpretation remains grounded. Scientific progress becomes possible because reasoning remains disciplined.

When this structure is not maintained, the failure is predictable. Distinctions collapse. Weak domains override strong ones. Interpretation expands beyond its limits. The boundary between knowledge and speculation dissolves.

The result is not deeper understanding.

It is confusion.

This chapter establishes the conditions that prevent that outcome. It defines the structure within which claims must operate, the constraints that govern their validity, and the limits that cannot be crossed.

There are no exceptions to these conditions.

# **7\. The Established Truth Scale (ETS)**

The Established **Truth** Scale (ETS) constitutes the primary outcome-oriented dimension of the **ATLAS** framework. While the other **ATLAS** scales evaluate how theories behave methodologically and how they integrate within the broader architecture of knowledge, ETS evaluates whether a theory has successfully demonstrated explanatory accuracy through convergent evidence and rational justification. It measures the degree to which a theory’s central claims correspond reliably to observable or inferable features of reality within its defined domain.

ETS is designed to distinguish between different stages of epistemic maturity, ranging from speculative or indeterminate theoretical claims to frameworks that have achieved robust evidentiary confirmation and comprehensive mechanistic understanding. The scale recognizes that theoretical knowledge develops progressively rather than instantaneously. Theories typically evolve from early conceptual formulation through increasing levels of evidentiary support, methodological testing, and explanatory refinement. ETS provides structured criteria for identifying where a theory resides along this developmental continuum.

A defining feature of ETS is its focus on evidentiary outcome rather than methodological posture or conceptual influence. A theory may demonstrate strong participation in scientific inquiry or exhibit elegant explanatory integration while still lacking sufficient evidence to justify epistemic confidence. Conversely, a theory that has achieved extensive evidentiary confirmation retains high epistemic standing even if certain methodological or integrative aspects remain under development. By isolating evidentiary confirmation as an independent evaluative domain, ETS ensures that theoretical legitimacy is grounded primarily in explanatory correspondence with reality.

The ETS framework is also structured around the Closure Layer Model introduced in the previous section. The scale distinguishes between phenomenon closure, where the existence of a phenomenon has been reliably established, and mechanistic closure, where the underlying causal processes responsible for the phenomenon are sufficiently mapped and understood. This layered structure allows ETS to evaluate theoretical completion without requiring unrealistic standards of absolute finality, while still providing rigorous criteria for identifying when a theory has achieved stable explanatory reliability.

The sections that follow will define each stage of the Established **Truth** Scale in detail, outlining the criteria required for a theory to advance through successive levels of evidentiary confirmation. These criteria form the foundation of ATLAS’s assessment of epistemic outcome and provide the primary anchor for the framework’s overall evaluation of theoretical legitimacy.

## **Purpose and Scope of ETS**

The Established **Truth** Scale (ETS) functions as the primary evaluative domain within the **ATLAS** framework for assessing what is known. Its purpose is to measure the degree to which a theory’s central explanatory claims have been confirmed through convergent **evidentiary** and rational evidence. ETS evaluates whether a theory accurately describes or explains a phenomenon within its intended domain and distinguishes between speculative, developing, and well-established explanatory frameworks. By focusing specifically on evidentiary confirmation, ETS provides a structured method for determining the epistemic reliability of theoretical claims.

At its core, ETS measures the stability and strength of explanatory knowledge. The scale identifies whether a theory has moved beyond preliminary or conceptual formulation and achieved levels of confirmation that justify increasing degrees of epistemic confidence. This includes evaluating the consistency of **evidentiary findings**, the reproducibility of supporting evidence, and the degree to which independent lines of investigation converge on the same explanatory conclusions. ETS therefore assesses not merely whether evidence exists, but whether that evidence collectively establishes a reliable correspondence between the theory and observable or inferable features of reality.

A central feature of ETS is its independence from methodological behavior. While scientific methodology provides the procedures through which theories are tested and refined, methodological rigor alone does not determine whether a theory is true. A theory may adhere to rigorous scientific standards by generating testable hypotheses, participating in peer review, and inviting **evidentiary scrutiny**, yet still fail evidentiary testing. Conversely, a theory may demonstrate evidentiary correspondence with observed phenomena even while certain aspects of its methodological articulation remain under development. ETS therefore evaluates the outcome of evidentiary testing rather than the procedural processes through which testing occurs. Methodological engagement is evaluated separately within the Scientific **Engagement** Scale, allowing each dimension to be assessed without conceptual overlap.

ETS is also independent from explanatory integration. A theory’s ability to harmonize with broader knowledge systems, unify multiple domains, or resolve conceptual tensions represents an important dimension of theoretical legitimacy. However, integration alone does not establish evidentiary accuracy. A theory may offer a conceptually elegant and highly integrative framework while lacking sufficient evidentiary support. Conversely, a theory may demonstrate strong evidentiary confirmation within a defined domain while remaining limited in its systemic integration across disciplines. By maintaining independence from explanatory integration, ETS ensures that evidentiary confirmation remains the central criterion for assessing whether a theory accurately describes reality.

The scope of ETS is therefore deliberately focused and narrowly defined. It evaluates the degree to which explanatory claims have been established as reliable knowledge within their domain, without incorporating considerations related to methodological sophistication, conceptual integration, ethical consequences, cultural influence, or practical utility. This focused scope preserves the clarity of epistemic evaluation and prevents the conflation of evidentiary confirmation with other important but distinct evaluative domains.

By isolating the measurement of what is known from how theories are tested or how they interact with broader knowledge systems, ETS provides the foundational anchor for the **ATLAS** framework. It establishes a structured continuum through which theories can be evaluated according to their evidentiary maturity, ensuring that theoretical legitimacy is grounded in demonstrable explanatory reliability.

## **Ontological Commitment Distinction**

The Established **Truth** Scale distinguishes between theories that explain relationships among already confirmed entities and theories that posit the existence of new, unconfirmed entities in order to explain observed phenomena.

A theory may achieve strong evidentiary support by accurately modeling relationships between known entities. In such cases, explanatory success increases confidence in the model without introducing new ontological commitments. These theories may achieve higher levels of truth establishment when their explanatory claims are supported by convergent evidence.

By contrast, some theories introduce new entities or structures that have not been independently confirmed in order to account for observed data. In these cases, the explanatory success of the model does not, by itself, establish the existence of the proposed entity. The theory remains dependent on an unverified ontological assumption.

This distinction can be clarified through comparison. The heliocentric model of the solar system explains the motion of the Earth relative to the Sun. Both the Earth and the Sun are independently observable and well-established entities. The theory does not require the introduction of a new, unconfirmed entity in order to account for observed phenomena. Instead, it explains how known objects relate to one another. As a result, its explanatory success contributes directly to its level of truth establishment.

By contrast, some theoretical frameworks introduce new entities in order to account for observed discrepancies. For example, the hypothesis of a proposed form of non-luminous matter (Dark Matter) has been used to explain gravitational effects that cannot be accounted for by observable mass alone. In this case, the observed phenomena are real and well-supported, but the existence of the proposed entity remains unconfirmed. The theory therefore relies on an additional ontological commitment that has not yet been independently established.

The Established **Truth** Scale therefore treats these cases differently. Theories that rely on unconfirmed entities are limited in their maximum level of truth establishment until independent evidence confirms the existence of those entities. Explanatory adequacy alone is not sufficient to establish ontological reality.

This distinction prevents placeholder constructs, inferred variables, or mathematically convenient entities from being misclassified as established features of reality in the absence of direct or convergent evidentiary confirmation.

## **ETS Stage Qualification Criteria and Illustrative Examples**

The Established **Truth** Scale is not intended to function as a subjective interpretive guide, but as an operational evaluative instrument. In order to preserve consistency, transparency, and reproducibility, each stage of the scale is governed by qualification criteria that must be satisfied for a theory to be assigned to that level. These criteria function as gating conditions rather than heuristic suggestions. A theory qualifies for a given ETS stage only when the required evidentiary thresholds have been satisfied according to available evidentiary and rational justification.

These gating criteria are designed to minimize ambiguity by identifying observable or documentable indicators of epistemic maturity. While theoretical evaluation inevitably involves expert judgment, the use of explicit qualification criteria ensures that such judgment remains constrained by shared evaluative standards. The following subsections present the operational criteria for each ETS stage, followed by illustrative examples demonstrating how these criteria apply to real theoretical frameworks.

## **ETS \+4 Complete Mechanistic Theory**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as a complete mechanistic theory when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory demonstrates phenomenon closure through convergent evidentiary confirmation across multiple independent investigative methods.  
* The theory provides a causal explanatory model that identifies the functional processes responsible for producing the phenomenon.  
* The causal mechanisms identified by the theory generate reliable predictive outcomes across varied evidentiary conditions within the domain of application.  
* The explanatory model remains stable under adversarial testing, replication, and cross-disciplinary scrutiny.  
* No competing mechanistic framework currently exists that explains the same phenomenon with equal or superior predictive and explanatory performance.  
* Remaining evidentiary uncertainties are limited to descriptive refinement, contextual variation, or parameter specification rather than unresolved core causal architecture.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Complete mechanistic closure does not require exhaustive knowledge of every micro-level variable associated with a phenomenon. Instead, it requires that the core causal processes responsible for the phenomenon are sufficiently mapped to produce reliable explanatory and predictive stability. The presence of ongoing data collection does not disqualify a theory from achieving mechanistic closure so long as such data collection does not expose unresolved foundational explanatory gaps.

## **Illustrative Examples**

Examples of theories commonly understood to approach mechanistic closure include certain domains of classical genetics, where inheritance mechanisms have been mapped to identifiable molecular processes, and well-established domains of physical interaction such as the relationship between electromagnetic fields and charged particle behavior within classical and quantum electrodynamics. In these cases, evidentiary convergence, predictive stability, and mechanistic clarity collectively support classification at the highest evidentiary level, even while ongoing research continues to refine domain-specific detail.

## **ETS \+3 Proven Phenomenon**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as representing a proven phenomenon when it satisfies the following conditions:

* Multiple independent lines of evidentiary investigation confirm the existence of the phenomenon.  
* The phenomenon demonstrates consistent reproducibility across investigative methods or observational contexts.  
* Independent research programs converge upon the conclusion that the phenomenon exists and operates reliably within the domain of investigation.  
* Predictive or retrodictive success demonstrates that the phenomenon is not an artifact of measurement error, methodological bias, or interpretive ambiguity.  
* Reasonable alternative explanations denying the existence of the phenomenon have been disconfirmed by evidence or rationally.  
* Mechanistic explanation may remain incomplete or contested, but the existence of the phenomenon itself is no longer reasonably disputed.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Proven phenomenon status represents a high level of epistemic confidence regarding the reality of a phenomenon while allowing for continued theoretical investigation into underlying causal structures. This stage reflects closure regarding existence rather than closure regarding mechanism.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Biological evolution through natural selection represents a paradigmatic example of proven phenomenon closure. Convergent evidence from genetics, paleontology, comparative anatomy, and observed evolutionary processes demonstrates consistent evidentiary confirmation that species undergo adaptive modification across generational timescales. While ongoing research continues to refine detailed mechanisms of evolutionary change, the existence of evolutionary processes is firmly established through independent evidentiary convergence.

Similarly, the existence of gravitational attraction between masses represents phenomenon closure within physics, even while deeper theoretical investigation continues to explore gravitational interaction at extreme cosmological or quantum scales.

## **ETS \+2 Strongly Supported Theory**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as strongly supported when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* Multiple independent evidentiary studies provide consistent evidence supporting the theory’s central explanatory claims.  
* At least partial replication or independent confirmation of key findings has occurred across separate research programs or investigative methodologies.  
* The theory demonstrates meaningful predictive or explanatory success within its defined domain of application.  
* The theory is operationalizable, meaning that its central claims can be clearly articulated in a manner that allows evidentiary testing or rational evaluation.  
* Existing alternative explanations remain plausible but do not currently match the evidentiary strength or explanatory performance of the theory.  
* Outstanding uncertainties primarily concern scope, mechanism, or evidentiary completeness rather than the coherence or plausibility of the theory’s core explanatory structure.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Strongly supported theories represent mature and credible explanatory frameworks that have accumulated significant evidentiary validation but have not yet achieved the level of evidentiary convergence required for phenomenon closure. Theories at this stage typically function as leading explanatory models within their domains while continuing to undergo refinement, replication, and expansion.

Importantly, strongly supported status does not indicate provisional or speculative standing. Rather, it indicates that a theory has achieved substantial evidentiary grounding while remaining open to further stabilization through continued research. The transition from strongly supported theory to proven phenomenon typically occurs when independent evidentiary convergence eliminates reasonable dispute regarding the existence of the phenomenon described by the theory.

## **Illustrative Examples**

Theories addressing the role of neuroplasticity in cognitive and behavioral development provide examples of strongly supported frameworks. evidentiary research across neuroscience, psychology, and clinical intervention studies consistently demonstrates that neural structures and functional pathways exhibit adaptive modification in response to environmental and experiential influences. While the existence of neuroplasticity is widely supported, ongoing investigation continues to refine the mechanisms governing specific forms of neural adaptation and their interaction with developmental and environmental variables.

Similarly, certain contemporary models of climate dynamics operate as strongly supported explanatory frameworks. evidentiary observations across atmospheric measurement, oceanographic analysis, and climate modeling provide convergent evidence supporting the role of anthropogenic factors in global temperature and climate pattern change. Continued investigation seeks to refine mechanistic detail and predictive resolution while the core explanatory claims remain strongly supported by evidentiary evidence.

## **ETS \+1 Emerging Supported Theory**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as emerging supported when it satisfies the following conditions:

* Preliminary evidentiary evidence or rational analysis provides initial support for the theory’s explanatory claims.  
* The theory is conceptually coherent and sufficiently defined to allow evidentiary testing or structured rational evaluation.  
* The theory demonstrates early predictive or explanatory potential that distinguishes it from purely speculative or conjectural proposals.  
* Independent replication of supporting evidence is limited or absent, but available evidence does not currently contradict the theory’s central claims.  
* Significant evidentiary, methodological, or theoretical refinement is required before the theory can advance to higher levels of epistemic confirmation.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Emerging supported theories represent legitimate candidates for continued investigation and development. They typically occupy early stages of scientific or philosophical maturation, where conceptual structure and initial evidentiary findings provide justification for further research investment. Placement at this stage reflects evidentiary promise rather than epistemic stabilization.

Theories at this level frequently undergo rapid revision as additional evidence accumulates or as methodological refinement clarifies explanatory scope. Some emerging supported theories advance toward strongly supported status, while others may remain indeterminate or become debunked by evidence as investigation progresses.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Early-stage theoretical frameworks addressing the role of gut microbiome variation in complex behavioral or psychological outcomes provide examples of emerging supported theories. Preliminary evidentiary research suggests correlations between microbiome composition and cognitive or emotional functioning. However, causal mechanisms, replicability across populations, and explanatory scope remain under active investigation, preventing classification at higher evidentiary levels.

Similarly, theoretical proposals concerning specific forms of quantum information processing in biological systems currently occupy emerging supported status. Preliminary evidentiary findings and conceptual models suggest potential explanatory relevance, but substantial additional evidentiary validation and mechanistic clarification remain necessary.

## **ETS 0 Indeterminate**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as indeterminate when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory is conceptually coherent and internally consistent enough to be meaningfully evaluated.  
* Available evidentiary or rational evidence is insufficient to confirm or refute the theory’s central explanatory claims.  
* The theory may be testable in principle, but practical or technological limitations prevent decisive evidentiary evaluation at the present time, or available evidence remains inconclusive.  
* Existing evidence neither provides strong support for the theory nor demonstrates reliable contradiction of its central claims.  
* The theory’s explanatory claims remain open to investigation without clear justification for positive or negative epistemic classification.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Indeterminate classification does not imply that a theory lacks intellectual value or investigative legitimacy. Many scientifically or philosophically important theoretical proposals occupy indeterminate status during early stages of inquiry or when they address domains that remain difficult to access through existing evidentiary methods. Theories at this stage frequently stimulate research programs, motivate technological innovation, and refine conceptual understanding even when definitive evidentiary evaluation remains unavailable.

Indeterminate status is therefore epistemically neutral rather than dismissive. It reflects the recognition that theoretical development often precedes the availability of sufficient evidence to support or refute explanatory claims. Theories at this level remain open to advancement toward supported or debunked classification as new evidence emerges or as methodological capabilities expand.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Certain cosmological theories addressing large-scale multiverse models illustrate indeterminate classification. These theories may be conceptually coherent and mathematically consistent while proposing explanatory structures that remain beyond current observational capability. While such models may align with existing theoretical physics frameworks, available evidentiary evidence does not currently allow decisive confirmation or debunking of their central claims.

Similarly, theoretical proposals concerning cyclical cosmological models, such as oscillating universe scenarios, often remain indeterminate. These frameworks offer coherent explanatory alternatives for cosmic development but remain limited by the difficulty of obtaining evidentiary data capable of conclusively verifying or falsifying their predictions.

## **ETS \-1 Debunked**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as debunked when at least one of the following conditions is satisfied:

* Convergent evidentiary evidence consistently contradicts the theory’s central explanatory claims across independent investigative methods.  
* The theory produces predictions that have been repeatedly falsified under reliable experimental or observational conditions.  
* Alternative explanatory frameworks demonstrate substantially greater evidentiary accuracy and predictive reliability while directly accounting for phenomena the debunked theory attempts to explain.  
* The theory relies on ad hoc modifications, unfalsifiable reinterpretations, or methodological insulation to preserve explanatory claims in the face of contradictory evidence.  
* The theory’s central explanatory claims conflict with multiple independently validated knowledge domains without providing extraordinary evidentiary justification.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Debunked classification indicates that a theory no longer maintains epistemic legitimacy as an explanatory account of reality. This classification does not negate the historical or developmental importance of debunked theories. Many such theories have contributed significantly to the advancement of scientific methodology, experimental design, and conceptual clarity by motivating investigation and revealing the limitations of earlier explanatory models.

Within ATLAS, debunked theories are recognized as having failed evidentiary evaluation regardless of their historical influence, cultural significance, or methodological participation. This classification reflects the framework’s commitment to evidentiary outcome as the defining criterion of epistemic legitimacy.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Flat Earth cosmology represents a clear example of debunked classification. Convergent evidentiary evidence from satellite observation, astronomical measurement, geophysical analysis, and navigational science consistently contradicts the claim that the Earth exists as a planar structure. Predictive failures and methodological attempts to preserve the theory through reinterpretation further reinforce its debunked status.

Similarly, theories proposing spontaneous generation of complex organisms without biological reproduction have been debunked through controlled experimental investigation demonstrating the necessity of reproductive biological processes for organism development.

## **ETS Operational Boundaries and Application Rules**

The Established **Truth** Scale is designed to function as a structured and repeatable evaluative instrument. In order to maintain consistency, transparency, and intellectual fairness, ETS requires explicit operational rules governing how theoretical classifications are applied. These rules clarify the scope of evaluation, define evidentiary standards, and establish safeguards against interpretive misuse. The purpose of these boundaries is not to introduce additional evaluative criteria, but to ensure that the stage definitions and gating requirements of ETS are applied coherently across domains of inquiry.

### **Unit of Analysis Rule**

ETS evaluates specific theoretical formulations rather than broad philosophical, scientific, or cultural labels. Many intellectual traditions contain multiple internal interpretations, some of which may produce testable claims while others remain purely conceptual, metaphorical, or normative. **ATLAS** classification applies only to clearly defined explanatory claims that assert correspondence with observable or inferable features of reality.

For example, a philosophical framework expressed as symbolic, metaphorical, or existential interpretation does not qualify for ETS evaluation unless it advances testable explanatory assertions. Conversely, when a theoretical tradition advances specific evidentiary or cosmological claims, those claims may be independently evaluated according to ETS criteria without requiring wholesale classification of the broader intellectual tradition. This rule ensures that ETS classifications remain precise, avoids strawman evaluation, and preserves fairness across diverse theoretical traditions.

### **Burden of Proof Rule**

Advancement within the Established **Truth** Scale requires positive evidentiary justification. Placement at higher ETS stages is granted only when qualification criteria are demonstrably satisfied through convergent evidentiary or rational support. In the absence of sufficient evidence to meet the gating criteria of a higher stage, classification defaults to the highest stage whose criteria have been fully satisfied.

This rule prevents premature elevation of theoretical frameworks based on speculative promise, conceptual appeal, or incomplete evidentiary support. It ensures that ETS functions as an outcome-based scale grounded in demonstrable epistemic reliability rather than aspirational or provisional credibility.

### **Evidence Quality Hierarchy Rule**

ETS classification recognizes that evidentiary strength varies according to methodological rigor, replicability, and convergence across investigative domains. Evidence supporting theoretical claims is evaluated according to its reliability, reproducibility, and independence from single-method or single-study findings.

Evidence demonstrating replication across independent research programs carries greater epistemic weight than isolated evidentiary findings. Similarly, convergence across distinct investigative methodologies strengthens evidentiary reliability by reducing the likelihood that findings result from methodological artifacts or observational bias. The progression of theories across ETS stages therefore depends not only on the presence of supporting evidence, but on the quality and convergence of that evidence across independent lines of inquiry.

### **Theory Revision and Movement Rule**

ETS classification reflects the best available evaluation of theoretical legitimacy based on current evidence and remains open to revision as new evidentiary findings, methodological advancements, or conceptual clarifications emerge. Theories may advance to higher ETS stages when new evidence satisfies qualification criteria previously unmet. Conversely, theories may decline in classification if new evidence undermines previously accepted explanatory claims.

This rule preserves the adaptive and self-correcting nature of scientific and philosophical inquiry. ETS classifications therefore represent structured but revisable assessments rather than permanent or dogmatic judgments.

### **Indeterminate Versus Debunked Distinction**

The Established **Truth** Scale maintains a strict conceptual boundary between indeterminate and debunked theoretical status. A theory classified as indeterminate lacks sufficient evidence to justify confirmation or debunking of its central explanatory claims. Indeterminate classification therefore reflects epistemic neutrality rather than evidentiary weakness or implausibility.

Debunked classification applies only when convergent evidentiary or rational evidence reliably contradicts the central explanatory claims of a theory. Untestability, technological limitation, or incomplete evidentiary development does not justify debunked classification. Likewise, conceptual speculation that remains inaccessible is not automatically considered false. This distinction ensures that ETS preserves epistemic humility while maintaining rigorous standards for evidentiary debunking.

### **Functional Role Within ATLAS**

Together, these operational rules provide the governance structure through which ETS stage definitions and gating criteria are applied. They preserve clarity regarding what constitutes evaluable theoretical claims, establish consistent evidentiary standards, and ensure that classification remains transparent, fair, and revisable. By maintaining these operational boundaries, the Established **Truth** Scale fulfills its role as the primary epistemic outcome measure within the **ATLAS** framework while supporting reliable multi-domain theoretical evaluation.

# **8\. The Scientific Engagement Scale (SES)**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale (SES) evaluates the methodological posture of theoretical frameworks by examining how they participate in the processes through which scientific knowledge is generated, tested, and refined. While the Established **Truth** Scale measures whether a theory accurately corresponds to observable or inferable features of reality, SES evaluates how a theory behaves in relation to the standards of scientific inquiry. This distinction is essential because theoretical legitimacy depends not only on whether a theory ultimately proves to be correct, but also on whether it engages responsibly with the methods through which reliable knowledge is established.

Scientific inquiry is characterized by structured procedures that allow theoretical claims to be tested, challenged, refined, and, when necessary, rejected. These procedures include falsifiability, evidentiary testing, peer review, replicability, and openness to revision in response to contradictory evidence. SES measures the extent to which a theory participates in these processes. A theory qualifies as scientifically engaged only to the extent that its claims can be tested, measured, or evaluated through these methods. A theory that actively invites evidentiary testing and methodological scrutiny demonstrates scientific engagement regardless of whether its claims ultimately prove correct. Conversely, a theory that cannot generate testable predictions or cannot in principle be evaluated through observation or measurement does not qualify as participating in scientific inquiry. Theoretical development, mathematical modeling, or academic attention alone does not constitute scientific engagement. Frameworks that avoid or reject these processes may retain conceptual appeal or cultural influence while lacking methodological legitimacy as scientific frameworks.

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale therefore distinguishes between epistemic outcome and methodological behavior. Theories may demonstrate strong scientific engagement while ultimately being incorrect or subject to evidentiary debunking, just as theories may achieve evidentiary confirmation despite limited early methodological participation. By evaluating scientific conduct independently from evidentiary confirmation, SES allows **ATLAS** to identify the procedural integrity of theoretical inquiry without conflating methodological participation with explanatory accuracy.

SES operates as a behavioral scale within the **ATLAS** framework, focusing on how theoretical claims are presented, tested, and revised within scientific contexts. It evaluates whether theories generate testable predictions, permit falsification, participate in critical peer evaluation, and respond constructively to evidentiary challenge. The scale also identifies theoretical frameworks that avoid or resist scientific scrutiny through methodological insulation or through active rejection of scientific standards as legitimate modes of knowledge evaluation.

The sections that follow define the stages of the Scientific **Engagement** Scale by identifying patterns of methodological participation that distinguish robust scientific programs from partial engagement, weak interaction, or explicit rejection of scientific inquiry. Through this structure, SES provides a systematic method for evaluating the procedural integrity of theoretical frameworks while preserving the independence of methodological behavior from evidentiary outcome and systemic integration.

## **Purpose and Scope of SES**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale (SES) evaluates the methodological behavior of theoretical frameworks by examining how they participate in the processes through which scientific knowledge is generated and refined. While theories are often judged primarily by whether they are ultimately correct, SES recognizes that the manner in which theories engage with scientific inquiry represents an independent and essential dimension of epistemic legitimacy. The scale therefore measures the extent to which theories submit their explanatory claims to evidentiary testing, critical evaluation, and methodological accountability.

SES focuses specifically on how theoretical claims are structured in relation to scientific investigation. This includes evaluating whether a theory generates testable hypotheses, permits falsification, participates in peer review, supports replication, and responds constructively to contradictory evidence. The scale identifies patterns of methodological participation that distinguish theories that operate as part of ongoing scientific research programs from those that maintain conceptual or cultural influence while avoiding scientific scrutiny. Only frameworks that generate claims capable of being tested, measured, or evaluated through scientific methods qualify as participating in scientific investigation. Frameworks that cannot be evaluated in this way fall outside scientific engagement regardless of their conceptual sophistication or academic attention. By examining methodological conduct independently from evidentiary outcome, SES provides a structured framework for evaluating the procedural integrity of theoretical inquiry.

A central purpose of SES is to distinguish between epistemic truth and scientific conduct. The Established **Truth** Scale evaluates whether a theory successfully corresponds to observable or inferable features of reality. SES, by contrast, evaluates whether a theory behaves in a manner consistent with the standards of scientific investigation. A theory may demonstrate strong scientific engagement by generating testable predictions, inviting falsification, and participating in evidentiary research even if subsequent testing ultimately disproves its central claims. Conversely, a theory may be evidentially correct while initially emerging from conceptual or observational traditions that did not fully conform to formal scientific methodology. SES therefore preserves the distinction between being true and behaving scientifically, allowing **ATLAS** to evaluate both dimensions without conflating them.

Scientific engagement strengthens theoretical legitimacy by increasing the reliability, transparency, and self-correcting capacity of theoretical inquiry. Theories that actively participate in evidentiary testing and methodological scrutiny provide clearer pathways for verification, falsification, and refinement. Such engagement supports cumulative knowledge development by allowing theoretical claims to be evaluated through shared evidentiary standards. However, scientific engagement does not guarantee epistemic accuracy. Theories that demonstrate rigorous methodological participation may ultimately fail evidentiary testing, while theories that initially lack methodological structure may later be reformulated into scientifically testable frameworks. SES therefore measures procedural integrity rather than evidentiary success.

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale operates independently from both the Established **Truth** Scale and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale. SES does not evaluate whether a theory is evidentially correct, nor does it assess whether a theory integrates successfully across multiple knowledge domains. Instead, SES isolates the methodological dimension of theoretical legitimacy by examining how theories engage with the standards of scientific inquiry. This separation allows **ATLAS** to identify theoretical frameworks that demonstrate strong scientific conduct even when evidentiary confirmation remains incomplete, as well as frameworks that may exhibit conceptual influence or systemic integration while avoiding methodological accountability.

By defining the procedural scope of scientific participation, SES provides a structured method for evaluating how theoretical claims interact with the practices through which reliable knowledge is established. This methodological evaluation complements the evidentiary and integrative dimensions measured by the other **ATLAS** scales, ensuring that theoretical legitimacy is assessed across distinct but interrelated domains of epistemic evaluation.

## **Scientific Engagement and Conceptual Activity**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale distinguishes between theoretical participation in scientific inquiry and mere conceptual, mathematical, or academic activity.

A theory may be widely discussed, mathematically formalized, or conceptually sophisticated without participating in scientific investigation. Scientific engagement requires that a theory generate claims that can be tested, measured, or evaluated through observation, experimentation, or systematic data analysis. The presence of academic attention or internal theoretical development does not, by itself, constitute scientific engagement.

At the same time, scientific engagement does not require direct observation of the entities proposed by a theory. A theory may participate fully in scientific inquiry if it generates testable predictions and is subject to empirical constraint, even when its core entities are not directly observable. What matters is whether the theory can be evaluated through its observable consequences.

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale therefore evaluates whether a theory is meaningfully constrained by evidence and capable of being tested or falsified, not whether it is directly observable or widely discussed.

## **SES Stage Definitions, Gate Criteria, and Illustrative Examples**

Scientific **Engagement** Scale classifications require that a framework be investigable through testable, falsifiable, or measurement-constrained methods. Theoretical development, mathematical modeling, or academic attention alone does not qualify as scientific engagement. If a framework cannot be tested, measured, or evaluated through scientific methods, it must receive SES \= 0\.

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale operates independently from both the Established **Truth** Scale and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale. SES does not evaluate whether a theory is evidentially correct, nor does it assess whether a theory integrates successfully across multiple knowledge domains. Instead, SES isolates the methodological dimension of theoretical legitimacy by examining how theories engage with the standards of scientific inquiry. This separation allows **ATLAS** to identify theoretical frameworks that demonstrate strong scientific conduct even when evidentiary confirmation remains incomplete, as well as frameworks that may exhibit conceptual influence or systemic integration while avoiding methodological accountability.

By defining the procedural scope of scientific participation, SES provides a structured method for evaluating how theoretical claims interact with the practices through which reliable knowledge is established. This methodological evaluation complements the evidentiary and integrative dimensions measured by the other **ATLAS** scales, ensuring that theoretical legitimacy is assessed across distinct but interrelated domains of epistemic evaluation.

## **SES \+3 Core Scientific Program**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as a Core Scientific Program when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory generates sustained research activity across multiple independent institutions or research communities.  
* The theory produces clearly testable and falsifiable predictions that are actively investigated through experimental or observational methods.  
* The theory has undergone extensive peer review and has demonstrated replication of key evidentiary findings across independent research programs.  
* The theory demonstrates ongoing methodological refinement in response to evidentiary results, including revision of models, experimental approaches, or predictive frameworks.  
* The theory contributes to the development or refinement of scientific tools, analytical methods, or investigative technologies within its domain.  
* The theory responds constructively to contradictory evidence by revising explanatory models rather than insulating itself from evidentiary challenge.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Core Scientific Programs represent theoretical frameworks that function as organizing foundations for sustained scientific investigation. This classification reflects methodological generativity, defined as the capacity of a theory to continuously produce testable research questions, methodological innovation, and structured investigative programs.

Core Scientific Programs do not require that all explanatory claims within the theory be fully confirmed. Scientific research programs frequently contain competing internal models or unresolved theoretical questions. SES classification evaluates the methodological behavior of a theory rather than its final truth status, which remains the domain of ETS classification. Theories that later undergo revision or partial debunking may nevertheless qualify as Core Scientific Programs if they continue to generate evidentiary research and methodological advancement.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Contemporary evolutionary biology functions as a Core Scientific Program by generating ongoing research across genetics, ecology, paleontology, and computational modeling.

Quantum field theory provides a foundational research program within modern physics, producing sustained experimental and theoretical investigation across particle physics and cosmology.

Large-scale climate science research programs also demonstrate this level of engagement through predictive modeling, atmospheric observation, and interdisciplinary environmental analysis.

## **SES \+2 Strong Scientific Engagement**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as demonstrating Strong Scientific Engagement when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory produces testable and falsifiable explanatory claims.  
* The theory has undergone formal peer review or equivalent academic evaluation.  
* The theory permits evidentiary testing and does not rely on unfalsifiable reinterpretation of contradictory evidence.  
* Independent research groups have conducted at least preliminary evidentiary investigation of the theory’s claims.  
* The theory demonstrates openness to revision or refinement in response to evidentiary findings.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Theories at this stage participate responsibly in scientific methodology while not functioning as dominant organizing research paradigms. Strong scientific engagement reflects adherence to scientific standards, including falsifiability, peer review, and evidentiary scrutiny.

Theories at this level often represent emerging research directions or minority explanatory frameworks within their fields. Strong Scientific Engagement does not require large-scale institutional research infrastructure or widespread disciplinary acceptance. The defining characteristic is consistent willingness to submit explanatory claims to evidentiary evaluation.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Emerging neuroscientific models addressing specific mechanisms of consciousness or cognition often demonstrate strong scientific engagement by producing testable hypotheses and participating in evidentiary investigation.

Competing cosmological models exploring variations of dark matter or dark energy frameworks may also qualify at this level when they remain testable and methodologically accountable within scientific discourse.

## **SES \+1 Partial Scientific Engagement**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as demonstrating Partial Scientific Engagement when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory presents explanatory claims that are sufficiently defined to allow testable or falsifiable evaluation, even if such evaluation is limited in scope or development.  
* The theory does not explicitly reject evidentiary evaluation or scientific standards of evidence.  
* The theory has undergone limited or preliminary academic critique, peer review, or isolated evidentiary testing.  
* Evidentiary investigation of the theory’s claims remains minimal, inconsistent, or methodologically underdeveloped.  
* The theory demonstrates conceptual openness to methodological refinement but lacks sustained research participation.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Partial Scientific Engagement reflects limited but recognizable participation in scientific inquiry. Theories at this stage frequently represent early-stage conceptual frameworks that possess testable structure but lack sustained evidentiary investigation or institutional research infrastructure.

Placement at this stage reflects methodological immaturity rather than epistemic illegitimacy. Many important scientific theories have initially occupied this stage before advancing toward higher levels of methodological integration.

Placement at this stage requires that the theory’s claims are in principle testable or evaluable through scientific methods. Theories that cannot be tested or evaluated do not qualify for this stage and must be classified at SES \= 0

### **Illustrative Examples**

Speculative psychological or sociological models proposing new explanatory mechanisms for complex behavioral phenomena may qualify as partially engaged when they provide testable conceptual frameworks but lack replication or sustained evidentiary investigation.

Early interdisciplinary proposals concerning emergent social or cognitive dynamics may also fall within this stage when methodological structures remain under development.

## **SES 0 No Scientific Interaction**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as demonstrating **No Scientific Interaction** when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory presents explanatory claims that reference evidentiary or natural phenomena.  
* The theory does not generate clearly defined testable or falsifiable predictions.  
* The theory lacks sustained peer-reviewed evidentiary investigation of its central claims.  
* The theory may incorporate scientific terminology or selectively reference scientific findings without producing structured evidentiary testing.  
* The theory does not actively resist scientific evaluation but does not meaningfully participate in scientific methodology.

### **Interpretive Notes**

No Scientific Interaction represents **methodological non-participation**, not methodological resistance. Theories at this stage neither engage scientific investigation nor attempt to insulate themselves from it.

Many philosophical, speculative, or interpretive frameworks operate in conceptual domains that intersect with science without functioning as evidentiary research programs. Classification at this level reflects the absence of scientific participation rather than a judgment about intellectual value, cultural significance, or conceptual depth.

This category distinguishes neutral non-participation from methodological insulation (SES \-1) and active rejection of scientific evaluation (SES \-2).

### **Illustrative Examples**

Philosophical interpretations of cosmology or consciousness that draw upon scientific discoveries while offering no testable predictions may qualify as exhibiting no scientific interaction.

Broad metaphysical or interpretive frameworks that reference scientific findings but do not generate evidentiary research programs or submit claims to methodological testing typically fall within this classification.

## **SES \-1 Methodologically Insulated Theory**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as Methodologically Insulated when one or more of the following conditions is satisfied:

* The theory presents claims that cannot be meaningfully falsified under defined observational or experimental conditions.  
* The theory responds to contradictory evidence through ad hoc reinterpretation that preserves core explanatory claims without generating new testable predictions.  
* The theory employs definitional flexibility or shifting conceptual boundaries that prevent evidentiary debunking.  
* The theory selectively incorporates supportive evidentiary findings while dismissing contradictory evidence without methodological justification.  
* The theory maintains rhetorical engagement with scientific discourse while avoiding participation in peer-reviewed evidentiary investigation or structured methodological testing.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Methodological insulation reflects structural avoidance of evidentiary accountability rather than early-stage theoretical immaturity. Emerging theories may initially lack evidentiary development while remaining open to methodological testing. Methodologically insulated theories demonstrate resistance to falsification or revision.

Theories at this stage may maintain cultural or conceptual influence while failing to participate in the self-correcting mechanisms characteristic of scientific inquiry.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Certain cosmological or metaphysical frameworks that reinterpret contradictory evidence as metaphorical, subjective, or epistemically inaccessible often exhibit methodological insulation.

Historical geocentric cosmology models that preserved explanatory claims through increasingly complex epicyclic modifications despite contradictory astronomical evidence also illustrate this classification.

## **SES \-2 Anti-Scientific Doctrine**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as Anti-Scientific Doctrine when one or more of the following conditions is satisfied:

* The theory explicitly rejects evidentiary testing or falsifiability as valid criteria for evaluating explanatory claims.
