* The theory asserts epistemic authority based on non-evidentiary sources such as revelation, tradition, or ideological doctrine while presenting claims about evidentiary reality.  
* The theory dismisses peer review, replication, or scientific critique as illegitimate or unnecessary.  
* The theory actively discourages evidentiary investigation or rational critique of its explanatory claims.  
* The theory presents evidentiary contradictions as irrelevant to explanatory legitimacy.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Anti-Scientific Doctrine classification applies only when frameworks present evidentiary or cosmological claims while explicitly rejecting scientific evaluation as a legitimate mode of knowledge assessment.

Such frameworks may retain cultural, symbolic, or existential value. SES classification evaluates methodological posture rather than cultural significance or philosophical meaning.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Young Earth creationist models that explicitly reject geological, astronomical, and biological evidence while asserting evidentiary cosmological claims often qualify as anti-scientific doctrines.

Explanatory systems that attribute natural phenomena to supernatural or ideological causes while rejecting evidentiary investigation as illegitimate may also fall within this classification.

## **SES Operational Boundaries and Application Rules**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale functions as a structured evaluative instrument designed to assess the methodological behavior of theoretical frameworks. In order to ensure consistent application across domains of inquiry, SES requires operational rules that clarify the scope of methodological evaluation, define evidentiary expectations for scientific participation, and distinguish between different forms of methodological posture. These rules establish shared standards for determining how theories engage with scientific inquiry while preserving the independence of SES from epistemic truth assessment and systemic explanatory integration.

### **Unit of Methodological Analysis**

SES evaluates specific methodological expressions of theoretical frameworks rather than broad intellectual traditions or generalized philosophical positions. Many theoretical traditions contain diverse internal interpretations that vary widely in their relationship to scientific methodology. SES classification applies only to clearly articulated explanatory claims that assert evidentiary or natural phenomena and can therefore be evaluated according to standards of scientific participation.

For example, symbolic, ethical, or existential philosophical interpretations do not qualify for SES evaluation unless they advance evidentiary explanatory claims. Conversely, when a theoretical tradition presents specific natural or cosmological explanations, those explanatory claims may be independently evaluated according to SES criteria without requiring classification of the entire tradition. This rule preserves precision in methodological assessment and prevents inappropriate generalization across internally diverse intellectual systems.

## **Burden of Proof for Scientific Engagement**

Advancement within the Scientific **Engagement** Scale requires demonstrable participation in scientific methodology. Theoretical frameworks qualify for higher SES stages only when they provide clear evidence of evidentiary testing, falsifiability, peer review, or methodological refinement. In the absence of such evidence, classification defaults to the highest stage whose qualification criteria are fully satisfied.

This burden of proof requirement prevents premature classification of theories as scientifically engaged based solely on conceptual compatibility with scientific language or speculative testability. SES classification requires observable or documentable evidence of methodological participation rather than aspirational or rhetorical alignment with scientific discourse.

## **Evidence Quality Hierarchy for Scientific Participation**

SES classification recognizes that participation in scientific inquiry varies according to the quality, consistency, and independence of methodological engagement. Evidence of scientific participation is evaluated according to the reliability of evidentiary testing, the presence of replication across independent research programs, and the degree to which theoretical claims are subjected to structured peer evaluation.

Isolated evidentiary studies, informal critique, or selective reference to scientific findings represent lower-quality indicators of methodological engagement. Sustained evidentiary investigation across independent research communities, systematic peer review, and documented methodological revision in response to evidentiary findings represent stronger indicators of scientific participation. Advancement across SES stages therefore depends not only on the existence of scientific interaction but on the rigor and consistency of that interaction.

## **Distinguishing Scientific Failure, Methodological Avoidance, and Anti-Scientific Rejection**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale maintains critical distinctions between different forms of methodological posture. A theory that participates fully in evidentiary testing but produces inaccurate predictions represents scientific failure rather than methodological illegitimacy. Such theories may retain high SES classification despite failing evidentiary evaluation because they remain methodologically accountable to scientific standards.

Methodological avoidance occurs when theories present evidentiary claims while structurally preventing meaningful falsification or revision. These theories maintain superficial scientific appearance while insulating core explanatory claims from evidentiary challenge. Methodological avoidance corresponds to lower SES classification because it undermines the self-correcting mechanisms of scientific inquiry.

Anti-scientific rejection represents explicit denial of scientific methodology as a legitimate standard of knowledge evaluation. Theories occupying this classification reject evidentiary testing, peer critique, or rational evaluation while asserting explanatory authority regarding evidentiary phenomena. This distinction ensures that SES captures meaningful differences between participating unsuccessfully in science, avoiding scientific accountability, and rejecting scientific methodology altogether.

## **Theory Revision and Movement Across SES Stages**

SES classification reflects current methodological behavior and remains open to revision as theoretical frameworks change their engagement with scientific inquiry. Theories may advance to higher SES stages when they develop testable predictions, participate in peer-reviewed evidentiary research, or demonstrate sustained methodological refinement. Conversely, theories may decline in SES classification if they adopt protective strategies that insulate explanatory claims from evidentiary testing or reject scientific evaluation entirely.

This rule preserves the adaptive and developmental character of scientific inquiry by recognizing that theoretical frameworks can evolve in their methodological posture over time.

## **Behavioral Scope of SES Evaluation**

The Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates procedural conduct rather than epistemic outcome. SES classification does not determine whether a theory is correct, nor does it assess whether a theory integrates successfully across knowledge domains. A theory may achieve high SES classification while remaining unsupported by evidence, just as a theory may achieve evidentiary confirmation despite limited early methodological participation. By isolating methodological behavior as an independent evaluative dimension, SES ensures that scientific conduct is assessed without conflating procedural integrity with evidentiary confirmation or systemic explanatory coherence.

Together, these operational rules provide the governance structure through which SES stage definitions and qualification criteria are applied. They establish consistent methodological evaluation standards, preserve clarity regarding what constitutes scientific engagement, and ensure that SES functions as a transparent and reproducible measure of theoretical participation in scientific inquiry within the **ATLAS** framework.

# **9\. The Explanatory Integration Scale (EIS)**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale (EIS) evaluates the systemic compatibility of theoretical frameworks within the broader architecture of established knowledge. Whereas the Established **Truth** Scale measures evidentiary confirmation and the Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates methodological conduct, EIS assesses how a theory fits within the interconnected network of validated explanatory domains. It measures whether a theory harmonizes with independently confirmed knowledge, unifies multiple fields of inquiry through demonstrated compatibility, or generates structural contradictions that undermine epistemic coherence.

Knowledge does not exist as isolated fragments. Scientific and philosophical domains form an interdependent explanatory system in which findings from one field constrain, inform, and reinforce findings from others. Theories that align with well-established bodies of knowledge strengthen their epistemic credibility by contributing to systemic coherence. Conversely, theories that require the rejection of multiple independently validated domains without extraordinary evidentiary support weaken their legitimacy by generating fragmentation within the knowledge structure.

EIS therefore evaluates the integrative strength of a theory across disciplinary boundaries. It examines whether the theory unifies findings from multiple domains through alignment with established knowledge, resolves conceptual tensions between fields in a manner consistent with validated findings, and maintains compatibility with established explanatory frameworks. It also identifies theories that produce unresolved contradictions, isolate themselves from  Cross Domain accountability, or depend on widespread rejection of validated knowledge without sufficient justification.

Importantly, explanatory integration is distinct from evidentiary truth and scientific conduct. A theory may be evidentially well supported within a narrow domain yet remain poorly integrated across disciplines. Conversely, a theory may demonstrate apparent conceptual integration while lacking sufficient evidentiary support or alignment with established knowledge. By isolating systemic coherence as an independent evaluative dimension, EIS allows **ATLAS** to assess whether a theory strengthens or destabilizes the broader architecture of knowledge without conflating integration with evidentiary confirmation or methodological participation.

The sections that follow define the stages of the Explanatory **Integration** Scale, identifying the criteria through which theories contribute to  Cross Domain coherence, remain localized within limited explanatory scope, or generate systemic incompatibility with established knowledge frameworks. Through this structure, EIS completes the triadic architecture of **ATLAS** by evaluating the structural role that theories play within the interconnected landscape of human understanding.

## **Purpose and Scope of EIS**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale (EIS) evaluates the degree to which theoretical frameworks demonstrate systemic compatibility across domains of established knowledge. While individual theories often originate within specific disciplines, they operate within a broader explanatory architecture in which validated findings from multiple fields interact, constrain, and reinforce one another. EIS measures how well a theory aligns with this interconnected structure by assessing whether it coheres with independently supported knowledge systems or produces unresolved contradictions across disciplinary boundaries.

Human knowledge develops through cumulative integration rather than isolated discovery. Advances in fields such as physics, biology, psychology, and sociology frequently depend on explanatory compatibility with findings from other domains. Theories that successfully integrate across disciplinary boundaries contribute to the stability and coherence of the knowledge system as a whole. By contrast, theories that conflict with multiple independently validated domains without sufficient evidentiary justification introduce structural fragmentation that weakens their epistemic credibility.  
 EIS therefore evaluates theoretical legitimacy not only through local explanatory success but through the capacity of a theory to function coherently within the broader network of established knowledge.

Integration strengthens theoretical legitimacy because  Cross Domain compatibility increases the reliability of explanatory claims. When independent research programs converge upon compatible conclusions, the likelihood that those conclusions reflect underlying features of reality increases. Theories that unify findings across disciplines through demonstrated alignment with established knowledge often reveal deeper structural relationships between phenomena, allowing knowledge to function as an interconnected explanatory system rather than a collection of isolated observations. EIS captures this dimension of theoretical strength by evaluating whether a framework contributes to systemic coherence or generates unresolved explanatory tension.

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale is distinct from both evidentiary confirmation and methodological participation. The Established **Truth** Scale evaluates whether a theory accurately corresponds to evidentiary or rational evidence within its domain of investigation. The Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates whether a theory participates responsibly in scientific methodology through falsifiability, peer review, and evidentiary testing. EIS, by contrast, evaluates whether a theory aligns with or destabilizes the broader architecture of validated knowledge. A theory may achieve strong evidentiary support within a narrow domain while remaining poorly integrated across disciplines. Conversely, a theory may demonstrate apparent conceptual integration while lacking sufficient evidentiary confirmation or compatibility with established knowledge. EIS isolates systemic coherence as an independent evaluative dimension, preventing integration from being conflated with either evidentiary accuracy or scientific conduct.

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale operates independently from the other **ATLAS** scales while remaining structurally complementary to them. EIS does not evaluate whether a theory is true or false, nor does it measure whether a theory participates in scientific methodology. Instead, it assesses whether a theory contributes to or undermines the structural coherence of the knowledge system. Only frameworks that demonstrate compatibility with established, evidence-supported knowledge systems qualify for positive integration classifications. Frameworks that lack evidentiary grounding or do not interact with established knowledge systems do not qualify as integrated regardless of conceptual scope or explanatory ambition. By isolating this dimension of evaluation, EIS completes the triadic structure of ATLAS, ensuring that theoretical legitimacy is assessed across evidentiary reliability, methodological accountability, and systemic explanatory integration.

## **Evidentiary Conflict and Structural Incompatibility**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale distinguishes between a theory that is contradicted by evidence within its domain and a theory that is structurally incompatible with established knowledge systems.

A theory may fail evidentiary evaluation while remaining conceptually compatible with the broader framework of established knowledge. In such cases, the theory does not require rejection of the underlying principles, methods, or structures of the domain in which it operates. It is simply incorrect. This constitutes evidentiary failure and is evaluated within the Established **Truth** Scale, not as a failure of integration.

By contrast, a theory exhibits structural incompatibility when it can only be maintained by rejecting well-established findings, principles, or methods across one or more domains of knowledge. In such cases, the theory cannot be reconciled with the broader explanatory system without undermining it. This constitutes a failure of integration and is reflected in negative EIS classification.

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale therefore evaluates whether a theory fits within the established structure of knowledge, not whether it is correct. Being false does not, by itself, imply a lack of integration. Negative integration scores are reserved for theories that require systemic rejection of established knowledge rather than those that simply fail to meet evidentiary standards within their domain.

## **EIS Stage Definitions, Gate Criteria, and Illustrative Examples**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale evaluates the degree to which theoretical frameworks demonstrate systemic compatibility across the broader architecture of established knowledge. Whereas the Established **Truth** Scale measures evidentiary confirmation and the Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates methodological participation, EIS evaluates structural coherence across disciplinary domains. The scale distinguishes between theories that unify knowledge across multiple fields, those that operate successfully within limited domains, and those that generate unresolved contradictions with independently validated knowledge systems. Each EIS stage includes a conceptual definition, operational gate criteria, interpretive clarification, and illustrative examples demonstrating how integrative compatibility influences theoretical legitimacy.

## **EIS \+3 Architectonic Integrator**

### **Qualification Criteria**

Architectonic means it has **multi-domain integration**.

A theory qualifies as an Architectonic Integrator when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory demonstrates coherent explanatory compatibility across multiple independent scientific or scholarly domains.  
* The theory provides unifying conceptual or structural principles that reveal shared explanatory relationships between disciplines.  
* The theory resolves previously existing conceptual tensions or contradictions between independently validated knowledge systems.  
* The theory maintains compatibility with established evidentiary findings across all domains it seeks to integrate.  
* The theory generates new interdisciplinary research programs or methodological approaches that extend beyond individual disciplinary boundaries.  
* No competing integrative framework currently provides equal or superior  Cross Domain explanatory unification.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Architectonic Integrators represent theoretical frameworks that provide large-scale structural unification across multiple independent domains of knowledge. This stage is not defined merely by multi-domain compatibility, but by integrative generativity, meaning the capacity of a framework to reorganize the conceptual landscape by identifying shared explanatory principles that apply across disciplinary boundaries.

Architectonic integration does not require elimination of disciplinary specialization. Instead, it provides an overarching explanatory architecture in which specialized disciplines function as interconnected components within a coherent knowledge system. Theories at this stage may operate at high levels of abstraction, but they must remain accountable to evidence across the domains they integrate. Integration must be achieved through explanatory coherence rather than metaphorical association or purely conceptual alignment.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Evolutionary theory, when applied as a unifying explanatory framework across biology, ecology, genetics, and behavioral sciences, can demonstrate architectonic integration by revealing common adaptive mechanisms across diverse biological phenomena.

Certain physical frameworks that unify fundamental interaction principles across particle physics and cosmology may also illustrate architectonic integration when they provide coherent explanatory continuity across multiple physical domains.

## **EIS \+2 Cross Domain Integrator**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as a  Cross Domain Integrator when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory demonstrates explanatory compatibility between two or more independent knowledge domains.  
* The theory provides explanatory mechanisms supported by evidence that link phenomena across disciplines.  
* The theory does not generate unresolved contradictions with established knowledge in the domains it integrates.  
* The theory supports interdisciplinary research or methodological collaboration across the domains it connects.  
* The theory remains accountable to evidence within each domain of integration.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Architectonic Integrators represent theoretical frameworks that provide large-scale structural unification across multiple independent domains of knowledge. This stage is not defined merely by multi-domain compatibility, but by integrative generativity: the capacity of a framework to reorganize the conceptual landscape by identifying shared explanatory principles that apply across disciplinary boundaries.

The term *architectonic* comes from philosophy and was used by Immanuel Kant to describe the systematic organization of knowledge into a coherent and unified structure. In the **ATLAS** framework, architectonic integration refers to theories that supply an overarching explanatory architecture capable of coordinating insights from multiple disciplines while remaining accountable to established evidence across the domains they integrate.

Architectonic integration does not eliminate disciplinary specialization. Instead, it provides a structural framework within which specialized fields function as interconnected components of a coherent knowledge system. Theories at this level may operate at high levels of abstraction, but they must remain grounded in established findings across the domains they integrate. Integration must be achieved through explanatory coherence rather than metaphor, analogy, or purely conceptual alignment.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Neuroscientific models that link cognitive psychology to neurobiology by connecting behavioral phenomena to neural mechanisms often demonstrate  Cross Domain integration.

Ecological models that connect evolutionary biology with environmental systems science can also qualify when they establish explanatory continuity between organism adaptation and ecosystem dynamics.

## **EIS \+1 Domain Integrator**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as a Domain Integrator when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory demonstrates explanatory coherence across multiple subfields or research traditions within a single disciplinary domain.  
* The theory resolves internal conceptual tensions or conflicting explanatory models within its field.  
* The theory integrates multiple lines of independently supported evidence within its domain into a unified explanatory framework.  
* The theory maintains compatibility with established knowledge in closely related disciplines, even if it does not actively integrate across those domains.  
* The theory supports ongoing research coordination within its field through shared conceptual or explanatory principles grounded in established findings.  
* The theory’s internal integration must be grounded in and consistent with established, independently validated findings within the domain.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Domain Integrators unify explanatory knowledge within a single discipline or specialized field of inquiry. This stage is defined by internal coherence rather than cross-disciplinary unification. Domain integrators consolidate fragmented knowledge into unified explanatory systems, coordinate diverse research findings, and stabilize conceptual frameworks within a domain.

Placement at this stage does not imply limited sophistication. Many broader integrative frameworks emerge from successful domain integration as models expand beyond disciplinary boundaries. Domain integrators may achieve high evidentiary support and strong methodological participation while remaining confined in integrative scope.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Plate tectonics within geology provides domain integration by unifying seismology, volcanology, and geophysical mapping into a coherent explanatory framework.

Unified models of behavioral conditioning within psychology can demonstrate domain integration by coordinating multiple experimental traditions within a single explanatory structure.

## **EIS 0 Standalone Theory**

Qualification Criteria  
A theory qualifies as a Standalone Theory when it satisfies all of the following conditions:

* The theory provides explanatory models for specific phenomena within a narrowly defined domain of inquiry.  
* The theory does not attempt to unify or reconcile findings across subfields or disciplines.  
* The theory remains compatible with established knowledge outside its domain but does not actively integrate with broader explanatory frameworks.  
* The theory contributes to specialized investigation without generating conceptual synthesis across research traditions.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Standalone theories function effectively within restricted explanatory contexts without attempting broader integrative synthesis. This classification reflects limited integrative scope, not theoretical weakness or epistemic illegitimacy.

Many scientific investigations rely on specialized models designed to address highly specific phenomena. Standalone theories frequently serve as building blocks for future integrative frameworks, providing domain-specific clarity that later contributes to  Cross Domain or architectonic integration.

This stage is defined by explanatory sufficiency within scope combined with minimal engagement in systemic integration.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Targeted pharmacological models explaining specific biochemical interactions within defined metabolic pathways often function as standalone theories.

Specialized atmospheric or climate submodels that address isolated phenomena may operate effectively within limited explanatory contexts while remaining components of larger integrative climate frameworks

## **EIS \-1 Fragmenting Theory**

## **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as Fragmenting when one or more of the following conditions is satisfied:

* The theory generates direct contradiction with established evidentiary findings from one or more independent knowledge domains without providing stronger evidentiary or explanatory justification.  
* The theory selectively accepts supportive findings while dismissing conflicting research without integrative explanation.  
* The theory requires compartmentalization of knowledge domains in order to preserve internal coherence, preventing meaningful  Cross Domain reconciliation.  
* The theory produces conceptual tension between disciplines that remains unresolved despite attempts at explanatory synthesis.  
* The theory isolates itself from interdisciplinary accountability by limiting evaluation to internally defined standards of evidence or interpretation.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Fragmenting theories generate unresolved contradiction or tension with established knowledge domains without providing sufficient integrative justification. This stage reflects structural incompatibility that produces disciplinary conflict without demonstrating explanatory superiority or integrative resolution.

Fragmentation differs from legitimate innovation. Novel theoretical proposals may conflict with established knowledge while providing strong evidentiary justification for revising existing frameworks. Fragmenting theories, by contrast, fail to demonstrate sufficient explanatory strength to justify rejecting validated domains. This classification therefore indicates unresolved systemic incompatibility rather than creative advancement.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Historical examples include biological theories that rejected genetic inheritance mechanisms while attempting to preserve alternative explanatory models without evidentiary justification.

Certain contemporary single-cause explanatory frameworks may also demonstrate fragmentation when they dismiss multidisciplinary evidence and generate unresolved contradictions across research domains.

## **EIS \-2 Systemically Incompatible Doctrine**

### **Qualification Criteria**

A theory qualifies as Systemically Incompatible Doctrine when one or more of the following conditions is satisfied:

* The theory requires rejection of multiple independently validated scientific or scholarly domains in order to preserve core explanatory claims.  
* The theory dismisses cross-disciplinary evidentiary evidence as irrelevant or illegitimate without providing extraordinary evidentiary justification.  
* The theory establishes internally defined epistemic standards that prevent evaluation according to shared knowledge criteria.  
* The theory presents explanatory claims about evidentiary reality while isolating itself from interdisciplinary verification.  
* The theory generates structural contradictions across knowledge architecture that cannot be reconciled through conceptual or evidentiary integration.

### **Interpretive Notes**

Systemically incompatible doctrines require large-scale rejection of established knowledge architecture in order to maintain their core explanatory claims. Unlike fragmenting theories, which generate unresolved tension with specific domains, systemically incompatible doctrines depend upon widespread dismissal of independently validated evidence, methodological standards, and interdisciplinary accountability.

This classification does not comment on cultural, symbolic, or existential value. Many doctrinal frameworks provide meaning or ethical orientation without functioning as evidentiary explanatory systems. Systemic incompatibility applies only when a framework asserts evidentiary or cosmological authority while requiring rejection of established knowledge systems.

### **Illustrative Examples**

Comprehensive cosmological doctrines that reject established astronomy, geology, and evolutionary biology while presenting alternative evidentiary world models often qualify as systemically incompatible.

Explanatory systems that attribute natural phenomena exclusively to ideological or supernatural causation while rejecting interdisciplinary evidentiary verification also demonstrate systemic incompatibility when presented as literal accounts of evidentiary reality.

## **EIS Operational Boundaries and Application Rules**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale functions as a structured evaluative instrument designed to assess the systemic coherence of theoretical frameworks across domains of established knowledge. In order to ensure consistent application, EIS requires operational rules that clarify the scope of integrative analysis, establish evidentiary expectations for  Cross Domain compatibility, and distinguish between legitimate theoretical innovation and unresolved structural contradiction. These rules provide the governance structure through which EIS stage definitions and qualification criteria are applied while preserving the independence of integrative evaluation from evidentiary confirmation and methodological participation.

### **Unit of Integration Analysis**

EIS evaluates specific explanatory claims or theoretical formulations rather than broad intellectual traditions or philosophical worldviews. Many theoretical traditions contain diverse internal interpretations that vary significantly in their relationship to  Cross Domain knowledge. EIS classification applies only to clearly defined explanatory frameworks that assert compatibility, unification, or contradiction across identifiable domains of established knowledge.

For example, symbolic or existential philosophical interpretations that do not assert evidentiary explanatory claims do not qualify for EIS evaluation. Conversely, when a theoretical framework presents integrative explanatory claims linking multiple scientific or scholarly domains, those claims may be independently evaluated according to EIS criteria without requiring classification of the entire intellectual tradition. This rule preserves analytic precision and prevents inappropriate generalization across internally heterogeneous theoretical systems.

### **Burden of Proof for Rejecting Established Knowledge**

Theoretical frameworks that conflict with independently validated knowledge domains bear the burden of providing extraordinary evidentiary and explanatory justification for such rejection. Advancement toward higher EIS stages requires demonstrable compatibility with established research across relevant domains. When a theory requires dismissal of validated knowledge without providing integrative explanatory resolution supported by strong evidentiary support, classification defaults toward lower EIS stages.

This rule prevents theoretical frameworks from achieving integrative legitimacy through selective rejection of inconvenient evidence or through internally defined epistemic standards that bypass cross-disciplinary accountability. Integrative advancement requires demonstrating coherence across knowledge systems rather than asserting independence from them.

### **Evidence Quality Thresholds for Integration Claims**

EIS classification recognizes that  Cross Domain integration requires robust evidentiary and conceptual support. Integration claims must demonstrate compatibility with established findings across the domains being connected. Evidence supporting integrative frameworks is evaluated according to the reliability of evidentiary convergence, conceptual coherence across disciplines, and the absence of unresolved contradiction between independently validated research programs.

Superficial conceptual association or metaphorical comparison between disciplines does not satisfy integration criteria. Advancement across EIS stages requires demonstrable explanatory linkage supported by evidentiary support or well-supported theoretical models grounded in established findings within each domain of integration.

### **Distinguishing Theoretical Novelty from Structural Contradiction**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale maintains a critical distinction between innovative theoretical development and systemic incompatibility. Scientific and philosophical progress frequently involves challenging existing explanatory frameworks through novel theoretical proposals. Legitimate theoretical innovation may initially conflict with established knowledge while presenting strong evidentiary justification or well-supported theoretical grounding for revising existing models.

Structural contradiction occurs when theoretical frameworks conflict with established knowledge without providing sufficient explanatory or evidentiary justification for revision. EIS classification therefore distinguishes between productive theoretical challenge and unresolved incompatibility by evaluating whether conflicting claims are supported by integrative explanatory strength capable of reconciling or replacing established knowledge.

### **Theory Revision and Movement Across EIS Stages**

EIS classification reflects current integrative compatibility and remains open to revision as theoretical frameworks evolve or as new evidentiary findings alter  Cross Domain explanatory relationships. Theories may advance to higher EIS stages when they demonstrate successful interdisciplinary integration, resolve previously identified conceptual contradictions, or generate new explanatory frameworks that unify independent research domains. Conversely, theories may decline in classification if new evidence reveals unresolved  Cross Domain incompatibility or structural contradiction with established knowledge.

This rule preserves the dynamic and developmental nature of knowledge integration by recognizing that theoretical frameworks may expand or contract in integrative scope over time.

### **Localized Scope Versus Systemic Incompatibility**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale distinguishes between limited integrative scope and structural incompatibility. Localized theories may operate successfully within narrowly defined research domains without attempting cross-disciplinary integration. Such theories do not incur negative EIS classification solely due to limited scope. Negative classification occurs only when theoretical frameworks actively generate unresolved contradictions across established knowledge domains or require rejection of validated research without sufficient justification.

This distinction ensures that EIS evaluates systemic fragmentation rather than penalizing specialized or narrowly focused scientific investigation.

### **Structural Scope of EIS Evaluation**

The Explanatory **Integration** Scale evaluates systemic explanatory coherence rather than evidentiary truth or methodological conduct. EIS classification does not determine whether a theory is correct, nor does it measure participation in scientific methodology. A theory may demonstrate strong evidentiary confirmation within a specific domain while remaining poorly integrated across disciplines. Conversely, a theory may propose integrative conceptual frameworks while lacking sufficient evidentiary support or methodological participation. By isolating systemic coherence as an independent evaluative dimension, EIS ensures that integrative legitimacy is assessed without conflating structural compatibility with evidentiary confirmation or scientific conduct.

Together, these operational rules provide the governance framework through which EIS stage definitions and qualification criteria are applied. They establish consistent standards for evaluating  Cross Domain integration, preserve clarity regarding what constitutes systemic compatibility, and ensure that EIS functions as a transparent and reproducible measure of theoretical coherence within the **ATLAS** framework.

# **10\. Overall ATLAS Scoring System**

The **ATLAS** Scoring System integrates the three independent evaluative scales, the Established **Truth** Scale, the Scientific **Engagement** Scale, and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale, into a structured composite assessment of theoretical legitimacy. While each scale measures a distinct epistemic dimension, the overall **ATLAS** score provides a concise summary of how a theory performs across evidentiary confirmation, methodological participation, and systemic coherence. The scoring system preserves the independence of each scale while allowing for transparent and reproducible synthesis into a unified evaluative profile.

Within ATLAS, epistemic status is determined exclusively by the Established **Truth** Scale. A theory is classified as proven, unresolved, or debunked based on its ETS placement. The composite score does not determine whether a theory is true or false. Instead, it provides secondary diagnostic context by summarizing how the theory performs across scientific engagement and explanatory integration once its evidentiary standing has been established.

The maximum possible **ATLAS** score reflects the highest achievable rating on each scale. ETS ranges from \-1 to \+4, SES ranges from \-2 to \+3, and EIS ranges from \-2 to \+3. When combined, the highest possible composite score is \+10. This score represents a theory that demonstrates mechanistic closure at ETS \+4, functions as a core scientific research program at SES \+3, and provides architectonic integration across knowledge domains at EIS \+3. The composite score therefore captures not only evidentiary confirmation, but also methodological rigor and systemic coherence at their highest levels.

The scoring structure incorporates a principled treatment of negative classifications. When a theory receives a negative score on any scale, that negative value is retained in the composite calculation. If a theory receives a negative score on the Established **Truth** Scale, positive scores from SES or EIS do not override or neutralize that epistemic failure. In such cases, only negative values are counted in the composite total. This rule prevents theories contradicted by convergent evidence from achieving artificial legitimacy through methodological participation or integrative appearance. Evidentiary outcome functions as the primary anchor of legitimacy within the **ATLAS** hierarchy.

Composite scoring does not collapse the three scales into a single undifferentiated metric. Each scale remains independently visible within the theory’s evaluative profile. The composite score serves as a summary index rather than a replacement for Scale level analysis. This preserves interpretive transparency and prevents reduction of multidimensional epistemic evaluation to a simplistic numerical ranking. Users can see not only a theory’s total score, but also how that score is distributed across evidentiary confirmation, scientific engagement, and explanatory integration.

## **Composite Score Function and Interpretive Limits**

The **ATLAS** scoring system produces a composite numeric score that summarizes a theory’s overall epistemic profile across the three **ATLAS** scales: the Established **Truth** Scale (ETS), the Scientific **Engagement** Scale (SES), and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale (EIS). The composite score ranges from \-5 to \+10 and reflects the combined contribution of evidentiary status, methodological engagement, and systemic coherence.

However, the composite score does not independently determine the epistemic status of a theory. Within ATLAS, truth status is determined exclusively by the Established **Truth** Scale. A theory is classified as proven, unresolved, or debunked based on its ETS placement. The composite score functions as a secondary summary that refines this classification rather than replacing it.

The primary role of the composite score is diagnostic. It provides a comparative measure of how a theory performs across multiple epistemic dimensions once its truth status has been established. This allows **ATLAS** to distinguish between theories that share the same ETS classification but differ in methodological rigor, explanatory maturity, or systemic integration.

For example, two theories may both be classified as proven at ETS \+3, indicating that the phenomena they describe are reliably established. However, one theory may demonstrate stronger scientific engagement or broader integration across knowledge domains. The composite score captures these differences without altering the underlying truth classification. Similarly, two theories may both be debunked at ETS −1, yet differ in their degree of scientific engagement or their compatibility with established knowledge. The composite score allows **ATLAS** to distinguish between scientifically engaged but ultimately debunked frameworks and those that are structurally non-scientific or incompatible with validated knowledge systems.

The composite score is therefore most informative when interpreted in conjunction with ETS classification. Comparisons between theories should be made within or alongside their ETS category, not across categories in isolation. A higher composite score does not imply that a theory is “more true” than a theory with a lower score if their ETS classifications differ. Truth status remains anchored to evidentiary evaluation.

Score ranges may provide general guidance about the relative maturity or weakness of theoretical frameworks, but they must not be treated as categorical indicators of truth. A theory with a lower composite score may still be fully established if its ETS classification indicates phenomenon or mechanistic closure. Conversely, a theory with a higher composite score may remain unresolved if its ETS classification does not demonstrate sufficient evidentiary support.

By distinguishing between categorical truth status and diagnostic profile, **ATLAS** preserves both clarity and nuance. The Established **Truth** Scale determines whether a theory is correct, while the composite score provides additional information about how well the theory functions within the broader epistemic landscape.

## **Scoring Exceptions: Safeguarding Epistemic Integrity**

**ATLAS** uses a structured scoring system to summarize the epistemic standing of theories. In most cases, scores across the three scales combine to produce a composite profile that reflects truth status, scientific engagement, and explanatory integration. However, two structural rules govern situations in which straightforward aggregation would produce misleading results.

These rules exist to preserve the hierarchy of epistemic evaluation. Truth status remains primary, and the scoring system must not allow methodological participation or conceptual coherence to create false legitimacy, nor allow limited scope or integration to undermine fully established knowledge.

The first rule, the Negative Override Rule, prevents theories that have been debunked from receiving artificial credibility through positive scores on secondary scales.

The second rule, the Closure Override Rule, ensures that theories which have achieved explanatory completion receive full epistemic confidence, regardless of their scope or level of integration.

Together, these rules protect the integrity of the **ATLAS** framework by ensuring that composite scores accurately reflect evidentiary reality rather than artifacts of aggregation.

## **The Negative Override Rule**

The **ATLAS** scoring system incorporates a Negative Override Rule that preserves the hierarchical role of evidentiary confirmation within overall theoretical evaluation. This rule establishes that when a theory receives an ETS score of \-1, indicating that the theory has been debunked through convergent evidentiary or rational analysis, positive scores from the Scientific **Engagement** Scale or the Explanatory **Integration** Scale do not contribute to the composite **ATLAS** score.

The purpose of this rule is to prevent theories that have been demonstrated to be false from achieving artificial epistemic legitimacy through methodological participation or systemic integration. A theoretical framework may participate actively in scientific investigation or may appear conceptually integrated across knowledge domains. However, if the core explanatory claims of the theory have been contradicted by reliable evidence, the theory cannot be rehabilitated as epistemically credible through procedural or integrative strengths alone. The Negative Override Rule ensures that evidentiary outcome remains the foundational criterion of theoretical legitimacy.

When the Negative Override Rule is applied, the composite **ATLAS** score is calculated by summing only the negative values present across the three scales. Positive SES or EIS values are excluded from the calculation when ETS equals \-1. This approach reflects the principle that once a theory has been debunked, its methodological engagement or integrative appearance may remain historically or analytically significant but cannot offset evidentiary failure.

The Negative Override Rule also allows **ATLAS** to distinguish between different types of debunked theoretical frameworks. Theories receiving composite scores between \-1 and \-2 typically represent frameworks that participated meaningfully in scientific inquiry but ultimately failed evidentiary evaluation. Such theories may have generated testable hypotheses, contributed to research, or stimulated scientific investigation while producing explanatory claims that were later contradicted by convergent evidence. These frameworks represent cases of scientific failure rather than methodological illegitimacy.

By contrast, theories receiving composite scores between \-3 and \-5 typically represent frameworks that not only fail evidentiary evaluation but also demonstrate methodological insulation, rejection of scientific standards, or systemic incompatibility with established knowledge. These theories often lack meaningful participation in scientific inquiry or require dismissal of validated knowledge systems in order to preserve their explanatory claims. **ATLAS** therefore distinguishes between theories that were scientifically engaged but debunked and those that were structurally non-scientific from their inception.

The Negative Override Rule therefore reinforces the internal coherence of the **ATLAS** scoring framework by ensuring that proven and debunked classifications remain anchored to evidentiary evaluation while allowing methodological and integrative factors to clarify the type and depth of theoretical failure.

## **The Closure Override Rule**

The **ATLAS** scoring system incorporates a Closure Override Rule that preserves the hierarchical role of explanatory completion within overall theoretical evaluation. This rule establishes that when a theory achieves mechanistic closure, indicating comprehensive causal understanding supported by convergent evidence and predictive reliability, the theory is assigned the highest level of truth establishment on the Established **Truth** Scale. In such cases, variation in the secondary scales does not reduce confidence in the theory’s correctness.

The purpose of this rule is to prevent theories that have achieved full explanatory maturity from appearing epistemically uncertain due to limited scope, domain specificity, or modest integrative reach. A theory may operate within a narrowly defined domain or contribute primarily to a specific scientific field. However, once the causal mechanisms governing the phenomenon are reliably understood and supported by convergent evidence, the theory has reached explanatory completion within its domain. The Closure Override Rule ensures that such completion is recognized as warranting maximum epistemic confidence.

When the Closure Override Rule is applied, the composite **ATLAS** score is set to the maximum value of \+10. Scores on the Scientific **Engagement** Scale and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale continue to describe methodological posture and systemic scope, but they do not diminish the epistemic standing established by explanatory closure.

This rule distinguishes explanatory completion from integrative breadth. Some theories achieve mechanistic closure within tightly bounded domains, such as specific biochemical processes or physical mechanisms. Their integration may remain localized, yet their explanatory reliability is complete. Conversely, other theories may exhibit broad  Cross Domain integration while still undergoing refinement at the mechanistic level. The Closure Override Rule ensures that completeness of explanation, rather than breadth of application, determines maximal epistemic confidence.

The Closure Override Rule also preserves the distinction between phenomenon closure and mechanistic closure. Phenomenon closure establishes that a phenomenon reliably exists and can be measured or detected. Mechanistic closure establishes how and why the phenomenon operates. A theory may achieve strong evidentiary support at the level of observable phenomena while continuing to refine its causal model. Maximum epistemic confidence is reserved for theories that demonstrate mechanistic completion, not merely observational confirmation.

Importantly, explanatory closure does not imply permanent finality. Scientific knowledge remains open to refinement, extension, and increased resolution. The Closure Override Rule recognizes completion relative to current evidentiary convergence and explanatory reliability, not absolute or universal finality. Subsequent refinements may expand scope, increase precision, or extend application without overturning the established causal framework.

By distinguishing explanatory closure from scope, integration, and future refinement, the Closure Override Rule preserves both epistemic rigor and conceptual clarity. It ensures that theories which have achieved comprehensive explanatory reliability are recognized as fully established while allowing secondary scales to continue describing methodological engagement and systemic reach.

## **Interpreting Score Patterns Under the Scoring Safeguards**

The Negative Override Rule and the Closure Override Rule clarify how composite scores should be interpreted when the scales produce asymmetric patterns. These safeguards ensure that the scoring system reflects evidentiary reality rather than surface impressions created by methodological activity or systemic scope.

Because the three scales measure different dimensions, theories often display uneven profiles. A framework that has been debunked may still exhibit strong scientific engagement. Historically significant theories frequently generated testable hypotheses, stimulated research programs, and advanced scientific methodology before being falsified. In such cases, a positive SES score reflects methodological participation, not epistemic validity. The Negative Override Rule ensures that procedural rigor cannot rehabilitate a disproven claim.

Conversely, a theory that has achieved explanatory closure may demonstrate a narrow domain of application and therefore show limited integration beyond its specific field. Mechanistic closure does not require broad  Cross Domain influence. A theory can be fully understood, predictively reliable, and evidentially complete within a tightly defined domain. In such cases, a lower EIS score reflects scope rather than uncertainty. The Closure Override Rule ensures that limited integration does not diminish confidence in established knowledge.

These patterns reveal the distinct diagnostic roles of the secondary scales. Scientific Engagement describes methodological posture. Explanatory Integration describes systemic reach and compatibility. Neither scale alters truth status once evidentiary evaluation has resolved it.  
 The composite score must always be interpreted relative to ETS classification. Identical composite scores may represent fundamentally different epistemic statuses if ETS placement differs.

Additional asymmetries follow from this structure. Debunked theories often exhibit negative integration scores because they conflict with established knowledge, while theories that achieve closure typically maintain strong engagement scores due to their successful participation in scientific inquiry. Theories still under investigation may display high engagement and integration while remaining unresolved on the truth scale, indicating active progress rather than established certainty.

Understanding these patterns prevents common misinterpretations. High engagement does not imply correctness. Broad integration does not substitute for proof. Narrow scope does not weaken established truth. Negative integration reflects contradiction, not merely lack of influence.

The scoring safeguards therefore function as interpretive guardrails. They ensure that composite scores cannot obscure the primacy of evidentiary evaluation while preserving the diagnostic value of methodological behavior and systemic coherence. By clarifying how asymmetric profiles should be read, **ATLAS** maintains both precision and transparency. The framework distinguishes between being scientifically active, being systemically influential, and being demonstrably correct. These are not the same achievement, and the rules ensure they are never confused.

The implications of this distinction can be illustrated directly:

### **Illustrative Examples**

A composite score may appear similar across frameworks while representing fundamentally different epistemic statuses:

* A theory with ETS \+3, SES \+2, EIS \+1 (Composite \+6) represents a proven phenomenon with moderate scientific engagement and limited integration.  
* A theory with ETS \+2, SES \+3, EIS \+1 (Composite \+6) represents a developing or partially established framework, despite having stronger scientific engagement. The identical composite score does not indicate equivalent epistemic status.  
* A theory with ETS −1, SES \+2, EIS −1 (Composite −2 under negative override) represents a debunked framework that still engages with scientific discourse, while a theory with ETS −1, SES −2, EIS −2 (Composite −5) represents a debunked and structurally non-scientific framework. Both are debunked, but differ significantly in methodological character.

These cases demonstrate that the composite score summarizes epistemic profile, but the Established **Truth** Scale determines categorical status.

## **Score Calculation Formula**

The **ATLAS** composite score is calculated by combining the results of the three independent evaluative scales: the Established **Truth** Scale, the Scientific **Engagement** Scale, and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale. The scoring formula is designed to preserve conceptual clarity while maintaining mathematical precision and reproducibility.

### **Standard Composite Calculation**

When a theory receives a non-negative ETS score, the **ATLAS** composite score is calculated by adding all three scale values:

#### **ATLAS Score \= ETS \+ SES \+ EIS**

This calculation applies to theories that remain epistemically viable or proven. Under standard calculation, all dimensions of theoretical legitimacy contribute to the overall evaluation. The resulting score ranges from 0 to \+10.

## **Negative Override Calculation**

When a theory receives an ETS score of −1, indicating that the theory has been debunked, the Negative Override Rule applies. Under this rule, positive SES and EIS values do not contribute to the composite score. When evidentiary debunking has occurred, only additional methodological or integrative failures affect the final score.

Under negative override, the composite score is calculated by adding only negative values across the three scales. This ensures that theories contradicted by convergent evidence cannot regain legitimacy through scientific participation or conceptual integration alone. Instead, the score reflects the depth and scope of the theory’s overall epistemic failure.

## **Conceptual Rule**

If a theory is debunked at the evidentiary level, **ATLAS** scores measure only the extent of additional methodological or systemic weaknesses rather than compensating strengths.

### **Closure Override Calculation**

When a theory achieves mechanistic closure, indicating that its causal processes are sufficiently mapped, predictively reliable, and evidentially stable within its defined domain, the Closure Override Rule applies.

Under this rule, the composite score is anchored at the maximum value of \+10, reflecting full evidentiary establishment. Variation in SES or EIS does not reduce the composite score once mechanistic closure has been achieved. Secondary scales continue to provide diagnostic information about methodological posture and systemic scope, but they do not diminish confidence in established explanatory completion.

This rule prevents domain scope from being mistaken for uncertainty. A theory may be fully understood within a narrowly defined domain without requiring broad cross-disciplinary integration. Mechanistic completion represents the highest level of explanatory maturity, and the composite score reflects that completion.

Conceptual Rule  
When mechanistic closure is achieved, the **ATLAS** score reflects full evidentiary establishment. Secondary scales describe scope and integration, not truth status.

### **Formal Expression**

If ETS ≥ 0 and mechanistic closure has not been achieved:

#### **ATLAS Score \= ETS \+ SES \+ EIS**

If ETS \= −1 (debunked):

#### **ATLAS Score \= ETS \+ min(SES, 0\) \+ min(EIS, 0\)**

If mechanistic closure is achieved:

#### **ATLAS Score \= 10**

### **Hierarchical Logic**

The scoring conditions reflect the evidentiary hierarchy embedded within ATLAS:

* Debunking overrides methodological participation and integration.  
* Closure overrides limitations in scope or integration.  
* Secondary scales provide diagnostic context, not compensatory authority.

This structure ensures that the composite score always reflects evidentiary reality rather than procedural activity or systemic breadth.

### **Interpretation Guidance**

The **ATLAS** composite score should always be interpreted alongside individual scale placements. The composite score summarizes overall epistemic standing but does not replace detailed analysis of truth status, scientific engagement, and systemic integration.

A debunked theory may show high scientific engagement while remaining epistemically invalid. A theory that has achieved closure may exhibit limited integration while remaining fully established. Theories still under investigation may display strong engagement and integration while remaining unresolved.

By combining conceptual rules with formal mathematical expression, the **ATLAS** scoring formula provides a transparent and rigorous method for synthesizing multidimensional theory evaluation into a unified composite legitimacy score.

## **Scope of Evaluation**

**ATLAS** evaluates frameworks that make claims about reality that can be meaningfully compared to evidence, logic, or established knowledge.

Frameworks are not excluded based on type. Instead, **ATLAS** distinguishes between different kinds of claims and evaluates how each performs across the three **ATLAS** scales.

The following categories define the primary types of claims evaluated within ATLAS:

### **Scientific Theory**

A scientific theory is a framework that proposes an explanation of reality and participates in systematic investigation.

It is characterized by:

* Testable implications or predictions  
* Engagement with evidence or observation  
* Responsiveness to falsification or revision

A scientific theory may be:

* Emerging  
* Strongly supported  
* Tully established  
* Or ultimately debunked

Its status as a scientific theory depends on its methodological engagement, not its level of confirmation.

### **Speculative Claim**

A speculative claim proposes an explanation of reality but lacks meaningful evidentiary accessibility.

It is characterized by:

* Limited or absent testability  
* Unclear or inaccessible methods of evaluation  
* Reliance on extrapolation, abstraction, or conceptual reasoning

Speculative claims may be logically coherent or mathematically structured, but they are not currently grounded in a testable evidentiary process.

### **Philosophical Claim**

A philosophical claim is a conceptual assertion about the nature of reality, knowledge, or existence.

It is characterized by:

* Reliance on logical reasoning rather than systematic testing  
* Attempts to explain fundamental aspects of reality  
* Potential overlap with scientific or religious claims

Philosophical claims may intersect with testable or evidentiary questions. When they do, they can be evaluated within **ATLAS** based on their correspondence with evidence.

### **Religious Doctrine**

A religious doctrine is a belief-based claim about reality grounded in tradition, authority, or revelation.

It is characterized by:

* Reliance on authority, tradition, or sacred texts  
* Resistance to revision based on evidence  
* Orientation toward meaning, identity, or worldview rather than systematic evidentiary evaluation

Religious doctrines may include claims about reality. These claims can be evaluated with **ATLAS** based on their correspondence with evidence.

### **Debunked Theory**

A debunked theory is a framework whose core claims have been contradicted by convergent evidence.

This classification is derived from the Established **Truth** Scale. A framework is considered debunked when it receives a negative ETS score.

### **Boundary of Evaluation**

**ATLAS** evaluates claims that make explanatory assertions about reality or that conflict with established knowledge.

Purely symbolic, metaphorical, or non-explanatory statements fall outside the scope of **ATLAS** evaluation.

### **Claim Type Distinction**

Before applying **ATLAS** scoring, each evaluation must identify whether the claim under consideration is primarily an **ontological claim** or an **explanatory claim**.

An ontological claim is a claim about whether a phenomenon, process, structure, or type of thing exists in reality. The core question is: Is this real? Examples include claims such as “evolution occurs,” “dark matter exists,” or “humans exhibit emotional regulation capacities.”

An explanatory claim is a claim about how a real phenomenon is structured, functions, develops, or is best explained. The core question is: Is this model of how it works accurate? Examples include claims such as “human needs follow a hierarchical dependency structure,” “matching instruction to learning style improves learning outcomes,” or “cognitive performance is best explained by a single general factor.”

This distinction is necessary because a real phenomenon does not automatically validate any particular explanatory model about that phenomenon. A framework may refer to a genuine aspect of reality while still presenting an incomplete, exaggerated, or false account of how that reality is organized or operates.

When a framework contains both kinds of claims, the ontological and explanatory components must be distinguished and evaluated separately. The existence of the phenomenon may warrant a higher ETS classification than the explanatory model built around it. **ATLAS** must not treat confirmation of a phenomenon as confirmation of every structural claim associated with it.

### **Relationship to ATLAS Scales**

The classification of a framework does not determine its truth.

Instead:

* The Established **Truth** Scale determines whether the claim is true, unresolved, or debunked  
* The Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates how the claim participates in systematic investigation  
* The Explanatory **Integration** Scale evaluates how the claim fits within established knowledge

**ATLAS** evaluates all eligible claims using the same criteria, regardless of their origin or category.

## **Units of Analysis**

**ATLAS** evaluates explanatory positions at multiple levels of granularity.

The primary unit of analysis is the **framework**, defined as a structured set of claims that together propose an explanation of some aspect of reality. Most **ATLAS** evaluations are conducted at this level.

**ATLAS** may also evaluate **theories**, which are frameworks that attempt systematic explanation, typically within a scientific context. A theory is therefore a specific type of framework characterized by its engagement with evidence and its participation in scientific investigation.

In addition, **ATLAS** can evaluate individual claims when they can be meaningfully isolated from a broader framework. A claim is a specific assertion about reality that can be compared to evidence, logic, or established knowledge.

Before evaluating an individual claim, **ATLAS** must determine whether the claim is ontological or explanatory in type. Ontological claims are evaluated in terms of whether the phenomenon or entity in question is real. Explanatory claims are evaluated in terms of whether the proposed structure, mechanism, dependency relation, or model accurately describes how a real phenomenon operates. This distinction prevents category error in cases where a framework refers to a genuine phenomenon but advances a weak or false explanatory model.

When evaluating claims at this level, the same **ATLAS** criteria apply, but the interpretation of ETS must remain appropriate to the type of claim being assessed. For ontological claims, ETS evaluates existence or reality status. For explanatory claims, ETS evaluates model accuracy or explanatory correctness. The Established **Truth** Scale determines whether the claim is supported, indeterminate, or contradicted. The Scientific **Engagement** Scale evaluates how the claim participates in systematic investigation, and the Explanatory **Integration** Scale evaluates how the claim fits within established knowledge.

Evaluating individual claims allows **ATLAS** to analyze specific components of broader frameworks without requiring evaluation of the entire system. However, care must be taken to ensure that the claim is sufficiently defined and contextually meaningful to be evaluated independently.

### **Claim Type Determination Rule**

All **ATLAS** evaluations must explicitly identify the type of claim being evaluated before applying any scale-based analysis.

Claims must be classified as either:

* **Ontological Claims**: assertions about the existence or non-existence of a phenomenon or entity  
* **Explanatory Claims**: assertions about the causal structure, function, or mechanism of a phenomenon

This distinction determines how the Established **Truth** Scale (ETS) is applied. Ontological claims are evaluated based on whether the phenomenon exists, while explanatory claims are evaluated based on how well the proposed explanation accounts for the phenomenon.

A single evaluation must not conflate ontological and explanatory claims. If a framework includes both types of claims, they must be clearly separated and evaluated independently or explicitly defined as a combined claim with a single evaluative target.

Failure to distinguish claim type results in ambiguous or invalid evaluation and is not permitted within ATLAS.

# **ATLAS Evaluation Template and Procedural Rules**

The following section defines the standard evaluation template used throughout ATLAS. While detailed, it is designed to ensure consistency and eliminate ambiguity in application.

The **ATLAS** framework is not only a conceptual model but a procedural system. To ensure consistency, transparency, and reproducibility, all evaluations follow a fixed structure and a set of deterministic rules. These rules govern both the format of evaluation and the conditions under which additional analysis is required.

**ATLAS** operates using two coordinated layers:

* A universal evaluation structure that is always present  
* A set of deterministic variation rules triggered by specific scoring outcomes

Together, these layers ensure that every evaluation is systematic, comparable, and resistant to interpretive drift.

### **Evaluation Sequence Requirement**

Every **ATLAS** evaluation must follow the same structure. The presentation structure is fixed and must not be altered or reordered. It ensures that all frameworks are analyzed using the same evaluation sequence.

## **Presentation Structure vs Evaluation Sequence**

**ATLAS** evaluations are presented with a summary at the top for clarity and accessibility. However, this summary is not the first step in the evaluation process. It is generated only after the full evaluation has been completed.

The evaluation sequence defines the required order of analysis and must be followed exactly. Each step builds on the previous one, and deviation from this sequence is not permitted.

The evaluation sequence is as follows:

1. **Describe the framework**  
2. **Identify the unit of analysis and the specific claim or claims being evaluated**  
3. **Classify each claim as ontological or explanatory**  
4. **Determine the Established Truth Scale** (ETS) **classification**  
5. **Evaluate the Scientific Engagement Scale** (SES) **and Explanatory Integration Scale** (EIS)  
6. **Calculate the composite score**  
7. **Interpret the result**  
8. **Apply all triggered variation rules** (Additional Analysis)   
9. **Construct and present the ATLAS Evaluation Summary** (displayed first, generated last)

The presentation structure consists of the **ATLAS** Evaluation Summary followed by the full evaluation in the order defined above.

## **Presentation Structure (Fixed Sections)**

Every evaluation must include the following sections in this exact order:

## **1\. ATLAS Evaluation Summary: Structure and Title Rules**

The **ATLAS** Evaluation Summary is a standardized output that presents the results of a completed evaluation in a concise and standardized format. Although it appears at the beginning of the evaluation, it is generated only after all analytical steps have been completed. The summary must reflect the final results of the evaluation without introducing new reasoning or interpretation.

#### **The Established Truth Scale (ETS) determines the epistemic status of the framework. All other scores provide diagnostic context.**

### **Title and Object of Evaluation**

Each **ATLAS** evaluation must clearly specify the object being evaluated. Many frameworks are commonly referred to by general labels, such as “emergentism,” “idealism,” or “attachment theory.” These labels often encompass multiple interpretations and do not, by themselves, define a single evaluable proposition.

For this reason, **ATLAS** requires that each evaluation distinguish between:

* The **framework or theory name**, and  
* The **specific claim being evaluated**

The framework name provides contextual reference, while the claim defines the precise proposition subject to evaluation. All scoring and classification apply to the specified claim, not to the framework label in its entirety.

The title of the evaluation must reflect this distinction where ambiguity is possible. When necessary, the title should include both the framework name and a concise formulation of the evaluated claim. This ensures that the object of evaluation is clearly defined and reproducible.

### **Summary Content Requirements**

The **ATLAS** Evaluation Summary must include the following elements in this order:

1. Framework or **Theory Name**  
2. **Specific Claim** Being Evaluated  
3. **Framework Status** (e.g. scientific theory, philosophical claim, speculative claim, religious doctrine)  
4. **ETS Classification** (Primary \- score and status label)  
5. **Closure Status** (if phenomenon closure or mechanistic closure)  
6. **Secondary Scale Scores** (SES, EIS)  
7. **Composite ATLAS Score** (Diagnostic)  
8. **Domain(s) of Inquiry**  
9. **Ontological Scale**(s)  
10. **Override Status** (Only when Negative Override Applied, Closure Override Applied)

Each element must be presented without justification or explanation. The summary is strictly a presentation of results and must not include reasoning, evidence, or argumentation.

### **Relationship to Full Evaluation**

The summary functions as a report of the completed evaluation. It does not replace or modify the detailed analysis that follows. All classifications and scores presented in the summary must be derived from the full evaluation and must remain consistent with it.

By standardizing the structure and title of the **ATLAS** Evaluation Summary, this rule ensures that all evaluations are clearly defined, consistently presented, and resistant to ambiguity. It prevents broad theoretical labels from being evaluated without specification and maintains alignment between the object of evaluation and the resulting classification.

## **2\. Framework Description**

A neutral description of the framework being evaluated.

It must include:

* the **core claims being made**  
* the **phenomenon or domain addressed**  
* **how the framework is typically presented**  
* the **claim type being evaluated** (ontological or explanatory)  
* **Domain and Ontological Scale Identification**  
* **Context of validity** \- Under which conditions or context the theory applies

Each evaluation must explicitly identify:

* the **primary domain**(s) of inquiry the framework operates within (e.g. physics, biology, psychology, sociology, philosophy, cosmology)  
* the **ontological scale**(s) the claim applies to (e.g. Nano, Micro, Intermediate, Macro, Meta)

This identification clarifies the scope and level of reality at which the claim is being evaluated.

All subsequent evaluation, particularly ETS classification and closure status, must be interpreted relative to the specified domain and ontological scale.

No evaluation occurs in this section.

## **3\. Established Truth Scale (ETS) Analysis**

This is the primary evaluative step.

It must include:

* **Classification**  
* **Justification**  
* **Closure Status** (if phenomenon closure or mechanistic closure)

The ETS analysis determines the categorical epistemic status of the framework. It must be completed independently before any evaluation of SES or EIS.

## **4\. Scientific Engagement Scale (SES) Analysis**

This evaluates how the framework interacts with scientific accountability.

It must include:

* **Classification**  
* **Justification**

This section evaluates methodological behavior, not truth status

## **5\. Explanatory Integration Scale (EIS) Analysis**

This evaluates how the framework fits within the broader knowledge system.

It must include:

* **Classification**  
* **Justification**

This section evaluates systemic coherence, not truth status.

## **6\. Composite ATLAS Score**

This section calculates the final score.

It must include:

* **Score breakdown** (ETS \+ SES \+ EIS)  
* **Explicit identification of any rule application** (negative override, closure override)

This ensures transparency in how the final score is derived.

## **7\. Interpretation**

This section explains what the score means.

It clarifies:

* The **epistemic status of the framework**  
* Its **level of credibility**  
* Its **role** within the broader knowledge system

Interpretation must follow from the scoring results and must not introduce new evaluative criteria.

# **Deterministic Variation Rules \- Additional Analysis**

In addition to the universal structure, **ATLAS** includes conditional sections called “**Additional Analysis**” that are triggered by specific scoring outcomes. These variations are not discretionary. They are automatically included when their conditions are met.

These sections provide additional explanatory depth while preserving consistency across evaluations.

## **A. High-Quality Scientific Framework Variation**

Trigger condition: **ETS ≥ \+3**

Section added: **Scientific Significance**

Purpose: **To explain the framework’s role in advancing scientific knowledge.**

Typical contents:

* **Explanatory scope**  
* **Predictive success**  
* **Role in scientific progress**  
* **Influence across disciplines**

### **Clarification of Threshold**

This variation is triggered at ETS ≥ \+3 because this threshold represents the transition from developing or unresolved theories to theories grounded in established phenomena.

At ETS \+3 and above, a framework has achieved sufficient evidentiary convergence to be considered reliably true within its domain. At this level, the question is no longer whether the theory is correct, but how it contributes to scientific understanding.

The Scientific Significance section is therefore required to articulate the broader impact of the theory, including its explanatory reach, predictive power, and role in shaping ongoing research.

This variation is not applied at ETS \+2 or below because theories at those levels have not yet achieved sufficient evidentiary stability to warrant evaluation of their broader scientific significance.

### **Illustrative Examples**
